        <sub>

        </sub>

        <span style="display:none">
         Y
        </span>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:WikiProject_Chemicals/Chembox_validation" title="Wikipedia:WikiProject Chemicals/Chembox validation">
         (what is this?)
        </a>
        <span class="reflink plainlinks nourlexpansion">
         <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Special:ComparePages&amp;rev1=595793408&amp;page2=Dopamine">
          (verify)
         </a>
        </span>
       </th>
      </tr>

     <p>
      <b>
       Dopamine
      </b>
      (contracted from
      <b>
       3,4-dihydroxyphenethylamine
      </b>
      ) is a
      <a href="/wiki/Neurotransmitter" title="Neurotransmitter">
       neurotransmitter
      </a>
      of the
      <a href="/wiki/Catecholamine" title="Catecholamine">
       catecholamine
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Phenethylamine" title="Phenethylamine">
       phenethylamine
      </a>
      families that plays a number of important roles in the human brain and body. Its name derives from its chemical structure: it is an
      <a href="/wiki/Amine" title="Amine">
       amine
      </a>
      that is formed by removing a
      <a href="/wiki/C-terminus" title="C-terminus">
       carboxyl group
      </a>
      from a molecule of
      <a href="/wiki/L-DOPA" title="L-DOPA">
       L-DOPA
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      In the
      <a href="/wiki/Brain" title="Brain">
       brain
      </a>
      , dopamine functions as a
      <a href="/wiki/Neurotransmitter" title="Neurotransmitter">
       neurotransmitter
      </a>
      a chemical released by nerve cells to send signals to other nerve cells. The brain includes several distinct dopamine systems, one of which plays a major role in reward-motivated behavior. Most types of reward increase the level of dopamine in the brain, and a variety of addictive drugs increase dopamine neuronal activity. Other brain dopamine systems are involved in motor control and in controlling the release of several other important hormones.
     </p>
     <p>
      Several important diseases of the nervous system are associated with dysfunctions of the dopamine system.
      <a href="/wiki/Parkinson%27s_disease" title="Parkinson's disease">
       Parkinson's disease
      </a>
      , a degenerative condition causing tremor and motor impairment, has been related to the loss of dopamine-secreting neurons in the midbrain area called the
      <a href="/wiki/Substantia_nigra" title="Substantia nigra">
       substantia nigra
      </a>
      . There is evidence that
      <a href="/wiki/Schizophrenia" title="Schizophrenia">
       schizophrenia
      </a>
      involves highly altered levels of dopamine activity, and the antipsychotic drugs that are frequently used to treat it have a primary effect of attenuating dopamine activity.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-1">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        1
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Attention_deficit_hyperactivity_disorder" title="Attention deficit hyperactivity disorder">
       Attention deficit hyperactivity disorder
      </a>
      (ADHD) and
      <a href="/wiki/Restless_legs_syndrome" title="Restless legs syndrome">
       restless legs syndrome
      </a>
      (RLS) are also believed to be associated with decreased dopamine activity.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2015)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Outside the nervous system, dopamine functions in several parts of the body as a local chemical messenger. In the blood vessels, it inhibits norepinephrine release and acts as a vasodilator; in the kidneys, it increases sodium excretion and urine output; in the pancreas, it reduces insulin production; in the digestive system, it reduces
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gastrointestinal_motility" title="Gastrointestinal motility">
       gastrointestinal motility
      </a>
      and protects intestinal mucosa; and in the immune system, it reduces the activity of lymphocytes. With the exception of the blood vessels, dopamine in each of these peripheral systems has a "
      <a href="/wiki/Paracrine_signalling" title="Paracrine signalling">
       paracrine
      </a>
      " function: it is synthesized locally and exerts its effects on cells that are located near the cells that release it.
     </p>
     <p>
      A variety of important drugs work by altering the way the body makes or uses dopamine. Dopamine itself is available for intravenous injection: although it cannot reach the brain from the bloodstream, its peripheral effects make it useful in the treatment of heart failure or shock, especially in newborn babies.
      <a href="/wiki/L-DOPA" title="L-DOPA">
       L-DOPA
      </a>
      , the metabolic precursor of dopamine, does reach the brain and is the most widely used treatment for Parkinson's disease. Dopaminergic stimulants can be addictive in high doses, but some are used at lower doses to treat ADHD. Conversely, many antipsychotic drugs act by suppressing the effects of dopamine. Drugs that act against dopamine by a different mechanism are also some of the most effective anti-nausea agents.
     </p>
     <p>
      Preparations containing dopamine hydrochloride are used in the treatment of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acute_heart_failure" title="Acute heart failure">
       acute heart failure
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kidney_failure" title="Kidney failure">
       kidney failure
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Myocardial_infarction" title="Myocardial infarction">
       myocardial infarction
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Septic_shock" title="Septic shock">
       septic shock
      </a>
      ,
      <i>
       etc.
      </i>
      and are on the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization%27s_List_of_Essential_Medicines" title="World Health Organization's List of Essential Medicines">
       World Health Organization's List of Essential Medicines
      </a>
      (WHO-EM), a list of medicines that are essential for a basic health system.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-2">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        2
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>

     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dopamine_pathways.svg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="335" data-file-width="479" height="154" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Dopamine_pathways.svg/220px-Dopamine_pathways.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Dopamine_pathways.svg/330px-Dopamine_pathways.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Dopamine_pathways.svg/440px-Dopamine_pathways.svg.png 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dopamine_pathways.svg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Major dopamine pathways. As part of the reward pathway, dopamine is manufactured in nerve cell bodies located within the ventral tegmental area (VTA) and is released in the nucleus accumbens and the prefrontal cortex. The motor functions of dopamine are linked to a separate pathway, with cell bodies in the substantia nigra that manufacture and release dopamine into the striatum.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Inside the brain, dopamine plays important roles in
      <a href="/wiki/Motor_control" title="Motor control">
       motor control
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Motivation" title="Motivation">
       motivation
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Arousal" title="Arousal">
       arousal
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cognitive_control" title="Cognitive control">
       cognitive control
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Reinforcement" title="Reinforcement">
       reinforcement
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Reward_system" title="Reward system">
       reward
      </a>
      , as well as a number of basic lower-level functions including
      <a href="/wiki/Prolactin#Effects" title="Prolactin">
       lactation
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sexual_gratification" title="Sexual gratification">
       sexual gratification
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Nausea" title="Nausea">
       nausea
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Dopaminergic" title="Dopaminergic">
       Dopaminergic
      </a>
      neurons (i.e., neurons whose primary neurotransmitter is dopamine) are comparatively few in number a total of around 400,000 in the human brain
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SchultzAnnRev_3-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-SchultzAnnRev-3">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        3
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
       and their cell bodies are confined to a few relatively small brain areas, but they send projections to many other brain areas and exert powerful effects on their targets. These
      <a href="/wiki/Dopaminergic_cell_groups" title="Dopaminergic cell groups">
       dopaminergic cell groups
      </a>
      were first mapped in 1964 by Annica Dahlstrm and Kjell Fuxe, who assigned them labels starting with the letter "A" (for "aminergic").
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-4">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        4
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In their scheme, areas A1 through A7 contain the neurotransmitter
      <a href="/wiki/Norepinephrine" title="Norepinephrine">
       norepinephrine
      </a>
      , whereas A8 through A14 contain dopamine. Here is a list of the dopaminergic areas they identified:
     </p>
     <ul>
      <li>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Substantia_nigra" title="Substantia nigra">
        substantia nigra
       </a>
       , a small midbrain area that forms a component of the
       <a href="/wiki/Basal_ganglia" title="Basal ganglia">
        basal ganglia
       </a>
       . The dopamine neurons are found mainly in a part of this structure called the
       <i>
        pars compacta
       </i>
       (cell group A8) and nearby (group A9).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bjorklund_5-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Bjorklund-5">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         5
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       In humans, the projection of dopamine neurons from the substantia nigra pars compacta to the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dorsal_striatum" title="Dorsal striatum">
        dorsal striatum
       </a>
       , termed the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Nigrostriatal_pathway" title="Nigrostriatal pathway">
         nigrostriatal pathway
        </a>
       </i>
       , plays a significant role in the control of motor function and in learning new motor programs.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Malenka_pathways_6-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Malenka_pathways-6">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         6
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       The name
       <i>
        substantia nigra
       </i>
       is Latin for "dark substance", and refers to the fact that the dopaminergic neurons there are darkly pigmented. These neurons are especially vulnerable to damage, and when a large fraction of them die, the result is a
       <a href="/wiki/Parkinsonism" title="Parkinsonism">
        Parkinsonian syndrome
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-7">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         7
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
     </ul>
     <ul>
      <li>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Ventral_tegmental_area" title="Ventral tegmental area">
        ventral tegmental area
       </a>
       (VTA), another midbrain area. The most prominent group of VTA dopamine neurons (cell group A10) project to the
       <a href="/wiki/Prefrontal_cortex" title="Prefrontal cortex">
        prefrontal cortex
       </a>
       via the
       <a href="/wiki/Mesocortical_pathway" title="Mesocortical pathway">
        mesocortical pathway
       </a>
       and another smaller group project to the
       <a href="/wiki/Nucleus_accumbens" title="Nucleus accumbens">
        nucleus accumbens
       </a>
       via the
       <a href="/wiki/Mesolimbic_pathway" title="Mesolimbic pathway">
        mesolimbic pathway
       </a>
       . Together, these two pathwars are collectively termed the
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mesocorticolimbic_projection" title="Mesocorticolimbic projection">
         mesocorticolimbic projection
        </a>
       </i>
       ;
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bjorklund_5-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Bjorklund-5">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         5
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Malenka_pathways_6-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Malenka_pathways-6">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         6
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       the VTA also sends dopaminergic projections to the
       <a href="/wiki/Amygdala" title="Amygdala">
        amygdala
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cingulate_gyrus" title="Cingulate gyrus">
        cingulate gyrus
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Hippocampus" title="Hippocampus">
        hippocampus
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Olfactory_bulb" title="Olfactory bulb">
        olfactory bulb
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bjorklund_5-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Bjorklund-5">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         5
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Malenka_pathways_6-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Malenka_pathways-6">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         6
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       Mesocorticolimbic neurons play a central role in reward and other aspects of motivation.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Malenka_pathways_6-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-Malenka_pathways-6">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         6
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       The nucleus accumbens is often considered to be the "limbic" part of the
       <a href="/wiki/Striatum" title="Striatum">
        striatum
       </a>
       . As such, it is the part of the striatum involved in the highest level aspects of motor control, which include motivation and decision-making. Thus, the role of the VTA in motivation and decision-making is structurally analogous to the role of the substantia nigra in low-level motor control.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DeLong_8-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-DeLong-8">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         8
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       In primates (i.e. monkeys and humans), the dopamine neurons from the regions of the substantia nigra and VTA project throughout most of the cortical mantle, with particularly dense innervation of the motor and premotor cortices. Thus, there are major species differences in cortical dopamine projections.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">
        <a href="#cite_note-9">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         9
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
     </ul>
     <ul>
      <li>
       The posterior
       <a href="/wiki/Hypothalamus" title="Hypothalamus">
        hypothalamus
       </a>
       . These dopaminergic cells (group A11) project to the spinal cord, and their function is not well established. There is some evidence that pathology in this area plays a role in
       <a href="/wiki/Restless_legs_syndrome" title="Restless legs syndrome">
        restless legs syndrome
       </a>
       , a condition in which people have difficulty sleeping due to an overwhelming compulsion to constantly move parts of the body, especially the legs.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10">
        <a href="#cite_note-10">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         10
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
     </ul>
     <ul>
      <li>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Arcuate_nucleus" title="Arcuate nucleus">
        arcuate nucleus
       </a>
       (cell group A12) and
       <a href="/wiki/Periventricular_nucleus" title="Periventricular nucleus">
        periventricular nucleus
       </a>
       (cell group A14) of the hypothalamus. An important projection from these dopaminergic neurons, termed the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Tuberoinfundibular_pathway" title="Tuberoinfundibular pathway">
         tuberoinfundibular pathway
        </a>
       </i>
       , goes to the
       <a href="/wiki/Pituitary_gland" title="Pituitary gland">
        pituitary gland
       </a>
       , where it influences the secretion of the hormone
       <a href="/wiki/Prolactin" title="Prolactin">
        prolactin
       </a>
       . Dopamine is the primary
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neuroendocrine" title="Neuroendocrine">
        neuroendocrine
       </a>
       inhibitor of the secretion of
       <a href="/wiki/Prolactin" title="Prolactin">
        prolactin
       </a>
       from the
       <a href="/wiki/Anterior_pituitary" title="Anterior pituitary">
        anterior pituitary
       </a>
       gland. Dopamine produced by neurons in the arcuate nucleus is secreted into the
       <a href="/wiki/Hypophyseal_portal_system" title="Hypophyseal portal system">
        hypothalamo-hypophysial blood vessels
       </a>
       of the
       <a href="/wiki/Median_eminence" title="Median eminence">
        median eminence
       </a>
       , which supply the
       <a href="/wiki/Pituitary_gland" title="Pituitary gland">
        pituitary gland
       </a>
       . The lactotrope cells that produce
       <a href="/wiki/Prolactin" title="Prolactin">
        prolactin
       </a>
       , in the absence of dopamine, secrete prolactin continuously; dopamine inhibits this secretion. Thus, in the context of regulating prolactin secretion, dopamine is occasionally called prolactin-inhibiting factor (PIF), prolactin-inhibiting hormone (PIH), or prolactostatin.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-prolactininhibition_11-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-prolactininhibition-11">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         11
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
     </ul>
     <ul>
      <li>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Zona_incerta" title="Zona incerta">
        zona incerta
       </a>
       . These cells (group A13) project to several areas of the hypothalamus, and participate in the control of
       <a href="/wiki/Gonadotropin-releasing_hormone" title="Gonadotropin-releasing hormone">
        gonadotropin-releasing hormone
       </a>
       , which is necessary to activate the development of reproductive systems that occurs following puberty, both in males and females.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-prolactininhibition_11-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-prolactininhibition-11">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         11
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
     </ul>
     <p>
      An additional group of dopamine-secreting neurons are located in the
      <a href="/wiki/Retina" title="Retina">
       retina
      </a>
      of the eye. These neurons are
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Retina_amacrine_cell" title="Retina amacrine cell">
       amacrine cells
      </a>
      , meaning that they have no axons. They release dopamine into the extracellular medium, and are specifically active during daylight hours, becoming silent at night. This retinal dopamine acts to enhance the activity of
      <a href="/wiki/Cone_cell" title="Cone cell">
       cone cells
      </a>
      in the retina while suppressing
      <a href="/wiki/Rod_cell" title="Rod cell">
       rod cells
      </a>
       the result is to increase sensitivity to color and contrast during bright light conditions, at the cost of reduced sensitivity when the light is dim.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
       <a href="#cite_note-12">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        12
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Outside_the_nervous_system">
       Outside the nervous system
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Dopamine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Outside the nervous system">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      Dopamine does not cross the
      <a href="/wiki/Blood%E2%80%93brain_barrier" title="Bloodbrain barrier">
       bloodbrain barrier
      </a>
      , so its synthesis and functions in peripheral areas are to a large degree independent of its synthesis and functions in the brain. A substantial amount of dopamine circulates in the bloodstream, but its functions there are not entirely clear. Dopamine is found in blood plasma at levels comparable to those of
      <a href="/wiki/Epinephrine" title="Epinephrine">
       epinephrine
      </a>
      , but in humans, over 95% of the dopamine in the plasma is in the form of dopamine sulphate, a conjugate produced by the enzyme
      <a href="/wiki/SULT1A3" title="SULT1A3">
       Sulfotransferase 1A3/1A4
      </a>
      acting on free dopamine. The bulk of this dopamine sulphate is produced in the
      <a href="/wiki/Mesentery" title="Mesentery">
       mesenteric organs
      </a>
      that surround parts of the digestive system. The production of dopamine sulphate is thought to be a mechanism for detoxifying dopamine that is ingested as food or produced by the digestive process plasma levels typically rise more than fifty-fold after a meal. Dopamine sulphate has no known biological functions and is excreted in urine.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eisenhofer_13-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Eisenhofer-13">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        13
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The relatively small quantity of unconjugated dopamine in the bloodstream may be produced by the
      <a href="/wiki/Sympathetic_nervous_system" title="Sympathetic nervous system">
       sympathetic nervous system
      </a>
      , the digestive system, or possibly other organs. It may act on dopamine receptors in peripheral tissues, or be metabolized, or be converted to
      <a href="/wiki/Norepinephrine" title="Norepinephrine">
       norepinephrine
      </a>
      by the enzyme
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dopamine_beta_hydroxylase" title="Dopamine beta hydroxylase">
       dopamine beta hydroxylase
      </a>
      , which is released into the bloodstream by the
      <a href="/wiki/Adrenal_medulla" title="Adrenal medulla">
       adrenal medulla
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eisenhofer_13-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Eisenhofer-13">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        13
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Some dopamine receptors are located in the walls of arteries, where they act as a
      <a href="/wiki/Vasodilation" title="Vasodilation">
       vasodilator
      </a>
      and an inhibitor of
      <a href="/wiki/Norepinephrine" title="Norepinephrine">
       norepinephrine
      </a>
      release.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Missale_14-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Missale-14">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        14
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      These responses might be activated by dopamine released from the
      <a href="/wiki/Carotid_body" title="Carotid body">
       carotid body
      </a>
      under conditions of low oxygen, but whether arterial dopamine receptors perform other biologically useful functions is not known.
     </p>
     <p>
      Beyond its role in modulating blood flow, there are several peripheral systems in which dopamine circulates within a limited area and performs an
      <a href="/wiki/Exocrine_gland" title="Exocrine gland">
       exocrine
      </a>
      or
      <a href="/wiki/Paracrine_signalling" title="Paracrine signalling">
       paracrine
      </a>
      function.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eisenhofer_13-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Eisenhofer-13">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        13
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The peripheral systems in which dopamine plays an important role include:
     </p>
     <ul>
      <li>
       <b>
        The immune system.
       </b>
       Dopamine acts upon receptors present on immune cells, especially
       <a href="/wiki/Lymphocyte" title="Lymphocyte">
        lymphocytes
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Buttarelli_15-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Buttarelli-15">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         15
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       Dopamine can also affect immune cells in the
       <a href="/wiki/Spleen" title="Spleen">
        spleen
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Bone_marrow" title="Bone marrow">
        bone marrow
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Circulatory_system" title="Circulatory system">
        circulatory system
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid10636479_16-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-pmid10636479-16">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         16
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       In addition, dopamine can be synthesized and released by immune cells themselves.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Buttarelli_15-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Buttarelli-15">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         15
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       The main effect of dopamine on lymphocytes is to reduce their activation level. The functional significance of this system is unclear, but it afford a possible route for interactions between the nervous system and immune system, and may be relevant to some autoimmune disorders.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid19896530_17-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-pmid19896530-17">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         17
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
     </ul>
     <ul>
      <li>
       <b>
        The kidneys.
       </b>
       Multiple types of dopamine receptors are present in cells of the
       <a href="/wiki/Kidney" title="Kidney">
        kidneys
       </a>
       . Dopamine is also synthesized there, by
       <a href="/wiki/Nephron" title="Nephron">
        tubule
       </a>
       cells, and discharged into the
       <a href="/wiki/Tubular_fluid" title="Tubular fluid">
        tubular fluid
       </a>
       . Its actions include increasing the blood supply to the kidneys, increasing filtration by the glomeruli, and increasing excretion of sodium in the urine. Defects in renal dopamine function can be produced by high blood pressure or by genetic problems, and can lead to reduced sodium excretion as well as hypertension.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid11566894_18-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-pmid11566894-18">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         18
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
     </ul>
     <ul>
      <li>
       <b>
        The pancreas.
       </b>
       The role of dopamine here is somewhat complex. The
       <a href="/wiki/Pancreas" title="Pancreas">
        pancreas
       </a>
       consists of two parts, known as
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Exocrine_component_of_pancreas" title="Exocrine component of pancreas">
        exocrine
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Islets_of_Langerhans" title="Islets of Langerhans">
        endocrine
       </a>
       . The exocrine part synthesizes enzymes and other substances, and secretes them into the
       <a href="/wiki/Small_intestine" title="Small intestine">
        small intestine
       </a>
       , where food is digested. One of the substances synthesized and secreted by the exocrine pancreas is dopamine. The function of this secreted dopamine after it enters the small intestine is not clearly established the possibilities include protecting the intestinal mucosa from damage and reducing
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gastrointestinal_motility" title="Gastrointestinal motility">
        gastrointestinal motility
       </a>
       (the rate at which food moves through the intestines).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rubi_19-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Rubi-19">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         19
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
     </ul>
     <dl>
      <dd>
       The endocrine part of the pancreas, also known as the
       <a href="/wiki/Islets_of_Langerhans" title="Islets of Langerhans">
        islets of Langerhans
       </a>
       , synthesizes a number of hormones, including
       <a href="/wiki/Insulin" title="Insulin">
        insulin
       </a>
       , and secretes them into the bloodstream. There is evidence that the
       <a href="/wiki/Beta_cell" title="Beta cell">
        beta cells
       </a>
       that synthesize insulin contain dopamine receptors, and that dopamine acts to reduce the amount of insulin they release. The source of their dopamine input is not clearly established it may come from dopamine that circulates in the bloodstream and derives from the sympathetic nervous system, or it may be synthesized locally by other types of pancreatic cells.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rubi_19-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Rubi-19">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         19
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </dd>
     </dl>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Cellular_effects">
       Cellular effects
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Dopamine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Cellular effects">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">

      <a href="/wiki/Dopamine_receptor" title="Dopamine receptor">
       Dopamine receptor
      </a>
     </div>
     <table border="10" class="wikitable" style="float:right; text-align:center;">
      <caption>
       Dopamine receptors in the mammal brain
      </caption>

      </tr>

         </sub>
        </a>
       </td>
       <td>
        <i>
         <a class="external text" href="http://www.genenames.org/data/hgnc_data.php?match=DRD2" rel="nofollow">

         </a>
        </i>
       </td>

         </sub>

         </sub>

       </td>
       <td rowspan="3">
        Decrease intracellular levels of
        <a href="/wiki/Cyclic_adenosine_monophosphate" title="Cyclic adenosine monophosphate">

        </a>
        <br/>
        by inhibiting
        <a href="/wiki/Adenylate_cyclase" title="Adenylate cyclase">
         adenylate cyclase
        </a>
        .
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td>
        <a href="/wiki/Dopamine_receptor_D3" title="Dopamine receptor D3">
         D
         <sub>
          3
         </sub>
        </a>
       </td>
       <td>
        <i>
         <a class="external text" href="http://www.genenames.org/data/hgnc_data.php?match=DRD3" rel="nofollow">
          DRD3
         </a>
        </i>
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td>
        <a href="/wiki/Dopamine_receptor_D4" title="Dopamine receptor D4">
         D
         <sub>
          4
         </sub>
        </a>
       </td>
       <td>
        <i>
         <a class="external text" href="http://www.genenames.org/data/hgnc_data.php?match=DRD4" rel="nofollow">
          DRD4
         </a>
        </i>
       </td>
      </tr>
     </table>
     <p>
      Like many other biologically active substances, dopamine exerts its effects by binding to and activating
      <a href="/wiki/Receptor_(biochemistry)" title="Receptor (biochemistry)">
       receptors
      </a>
      located on the surface of cells. In mammals, five subtypes of dopamine receptors have been identified, labeled D1 through D5. All of them function as
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/G_protein-coupled_receptor" title="G protein-coupled receptor">
       G protein-coupled receptors
      </a>
      , meaning that they exert their effects via a complex
      <a href="/wiki/Second_messenger_system" title="Second messenger system">
       second messenger system
      </a>
      . Glossing over the details, dopamine receptors in mammals can be divided into two families, known as
      <a href="/wiki/D1-like_receptor" title="D1-like receptor">
       D1-like
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/D2-like_receptor" title="D2-like receptor">
       D2-like
      </a>
      . The ultimate effect of D1-like receptors (D1 and D5) can be excitation (via opening of sodium channels) or inhibition (via opening of potassium channels); the ultimate effect of D2-like receptors (D2, D3, and D4) is usually inhibition of the target neuron. Consequently, it is incorrect to describe dopamine itself as either excitatory or inhibitory. Its effect on a target neuron depends on which types of receptors are present on the membrane of that neuron and on the internal responses of that neuron to cyclic AMP. D1 receptors are the most numerous dopamine receptors in the central nervous system; D2 receptors are next; D3, D4, and D5 receptors are present at significantly lower levels.
     </p>
     <p>
      The level of extracellular dopamine is modulated by two mechanisms: tonic and phasic dopamine transmission. Tonic dopamine transmission occurs when small amounts of dopamine are released independently of neuronal activity, and is regulated by the activity of other neurons and neurotransmitter reuptake.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">
       <a href="#cite_note-20">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        20
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Phasic dopamine release results from the activity of the dopamine-containing cells themselves. This activity is characterized by irregular
      <a href="/wiki/Pacemaker_action_potential" title="Pacemaker action potential">
       pacemaking
      </a>
      activity of single spikes, and rapid bursts of typically 26 spikes in quick succession.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21">
       <a href="#cite_note-21">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        21
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22">
       <a href="#cite_note-22">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        22
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Substantia_nigra_dopamine_system_and_motor_control">
       Substantia nigra dopamine system and motor control
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Dopamine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Substantia nigra dopamine system and motor control">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Basal_ganglia_circuits.svg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1453" data-file-width="1010" height="432" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Basal_ganglia_circuits.svg/300px-Basal_ganglia_circuits.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Basal_ganglia_circuits.svg/450px-Basal_ganglia_circuits.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Basal_ganglia_circuits.svg/600px-Basal_ganglia_circuits.svg.png 2x" width="300"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Basal_ganglia_circuits.svg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Main circuits of the
        <a href="/wiki/Basal_ganglia" title="Basal ganglia">
         basal ganglia
        </a>
        . The dopaminergic pathway from the
        <a href="/wiki/Substantia_nigra_pars_compacta" title="Substantia nigra pars compacta">
         substantia nigra pars compacta
        </a>
        to the
        <a href="/wiki/Striatum" title="Striatum">
         striatum
        </a>
        is shown in light blue.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Substantia_nigra" title="Substantia nigra">
       substantia nigra
      </a>
      is a component of the
      <a href="/wiki/Basal_ganglia" title="Basal ganglia">
       basal ganglia
      </a>
      , a group of interconnected structures in the forebrain and midbrain that play a central role in motor control. The precise nature of that role has been difficult to work out, but one popular line of thought describes it as "response selection". The response selection theory proposes that when a person or animal is in a situation where several behaviors are possible, activity in the basal ganglia determines which of them is executed, by releasing that response from inhibition. Thus the basal ganglia are responsible for initiating behaviors but not for determining the details of how they are carried out.
     </p>
     <p>
      Dopamine is thought to modulate the response selection process in at least two important ways. First, dopamine sets the "effort threshold" for initiating behaviors. The higher the level of dopamine activity, the lower the impetus required to evoke a given behavior. As a consequence, high levels of dopamine lead to high levels of motor activity and "impulsive" behavior; low levels of dopamine lead to torpor and slowed reactions.
      <a href="/wiki/Parkinson%27s_disease" title="Parkinson's disease">
       Parkinson's disease
      </a>
      , in which dopamine levels in the
      <i>
       substantia nigra
      </i>
      circuit are greatly reduced, is characterized by stiffness and greatly reduced movementhowever, when people with the disease are confronted with strong stimuli such as a serious threat, their reactions can be as vigorous as those of a healthy person. In the opposite direction, drugs that increase the effects of dopamine, such as cocaine or amphetamine, produce heightened levels of activity, including at the highest levels
      <a href="/wiki/Psychomotor_agitation" title="Psychomotor agitation">
       psychomotor agitation
      </a>
      and stereotyped movements.
     </p>
     <p>
      The second important effect of dopamine is as a "teaching" signal. When a motor response is followed by an increase in dopamine activity, the basal ganglia circuit is altered in a way that makes the same response easier to evoke when similar situations arise in the future. This is a form of
      <a href="/wiki/Operant_conditioning" title="Operant conditioning">
       operant conditioning
      </a>
      , in which dopamine plays the role of a reward signal.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Anatomy_and_physiology">
       Anatomy and physiology
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Dopamine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Anatomy and physiology">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The anatomy of the basal ganglia is extraordinarily complex, and the role of dopamine there is correspondingly complex. On a macroscopic scale there is only one major dopamine projection, from the
      <a href="/wiki/Substantia_nigra_pars_compacta" title="Substantia nigra pars compacta">
       substantia nigra pars compacta
      </a>
      to the
      <a href="/wiki/Striatum" title="Striatum">
       striatum
      </a>
      , but the dopamine inputs contact multiple types of neurons and have several distinct effects on their targets, activating some via D1 receptors while inhibiting others via D2 receptors. A substantial number of dopamine inputs are delivered to the necks of
      <a href="/wiki/Dendritic_spine" title="Dendritic spine">
       dendritic spines
      </a>
      , where they are well-placed to exert a gating effect on specific synaptic connections, often arising from the cerebral cortex. There are two distinct pathways of signal flow arising from the striatum, known as the
      <a href="/wiki/Direct_pathway_of_movement" title="Direct pathway of movement">
       direct pathway
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Indirect_pathway_of_movement" title="Indirect pathway of movement">
       indirect pathway
      </a>
      . Dopamine is thought to promote action by upregulating the direct pathway while suppressing the indirect pathway.
     </p>
     <p>
      Many theoreticians believe that the mechanism underlying motor learning in the basal ganglia involves a form of
      <a href="/wiki/Long-term_potentiation" title="Long-term potentiation">
       long-term potentiation
      </a>
      that occurs in the
      <a href="/wiki/Striatum" title="Striatum">
       striatum
      </a>
      and is strongly modulated by dopaminein other words, a mechanism by which dopamine activity induces strengthening or weakening of synaptic connections inside the striatum.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">
       <a href="#cite_note-23">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        23
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Ventral_tegmental_area_and_reward">
       Ventral tegmental area and reward
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Dopamine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Ventral tegmental area and reward">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <table class="metadata plainlinks ambox ambox-content" role="presentation">
      <tr>
       <td class="mbox-image">
        <div style="width:52px">
         <img alt="" data-file-height="40" data-file-width="40" height="40" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Ambox_important.svg/40px-Ambox_important.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Ambox_important.svg/60px-Ambox_important.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Ambox_important.svg/80px-Ambox_important.svg.png 2x" width="40"/>
        </div>
       </td>
       <td class="mbox-text">
        <span class="mbox-text-span">
         This article
         <b>
          needs more
          <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Identifying_reliable_sources_(medicine)" title="Wikipedia:Identifying reliable sources (medicine)">
           medical references
          </a>
          for
          <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">
           verification
          </a>
          or relies too heavily on primary sources
         </b>
         .
         <span class="hide-when-compact">
          Please review the contents of the article and
          <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Dopamine&amp;action=edit">
           add the appropriate references
          </a>
          if you can. Unsourced or poorly sourced material may be
          <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Burden_of_evidence" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">
           removed
          </a>
          .
         </span>
         <small>
          <i>
           (June 2014)
          </i>
         </small>
        </span>
       </td>
       <td class="mbox-imageright">
        <div style="width:52px">
         <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rod_of_Asclepius2.svg">
          <img alt="Rod of Asclepius2.svg" data-file-height="804" data-file-width="251" height="40" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Rod_of_Asclepius2.svg/12px-Rod_of_Asclepius2.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Rod_of_Asclepius2.svg/18px-Rod_of_Asclepius2.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Rod_of_Asclepius2.svg/25px-Rod_of_Asclepius2.svg.png 2x" width="12"/>
         </a>
        </div>
       </td>
      </tr>
     </table>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Ventral_tegmental_area" title="Ventral tegmental area">
       ventral tegmental area
      </a>
      (VTA) contains the largest group of dopamine neurons in the human brain. They project to numerous brain areas, but the two largest projections are the
      <a href="/wiki/Mesolimbic_pathway" title="Mesolimbic pathway">
       mesolimbic pathway
      </a>
      , which targets the
      <a href="/wiki/Nucleus_accumbens" title="Nucleus accumbens">
       nucleus accumbens
      </a>
      and other
      <a href="/wiki/Limbic_system" title="Limbic system">
       limbic
      </a>
      structures, and the
      <a href="/wiki/Mesocortical_pathway" title="Mesocortical pathway">
       mesocortical pathway
      </a>
      , which targets the
      <a href="/wiki/Prefrontal_cortex" title="Prefrontal cortex">
       prefrontal
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Insular_cortex" title="Insular cortex">
       insular
      </a>
      parts of the cerebral cortex.
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Reward">
       Reward
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Dopamine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Reward">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      The VTA dopamine system is strongly associated with the
      <a href="/wiki/Reward_system" title="Reward system">
       reward system
      </a>
      of the brain. Dopamine is released in areas such as the
      <a href="/wiki/Nucleus_accumbens" title="Nucleus accumbens">
       nucleus accumbens
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Prefrontal_cortex" title="Prefrontal cortex">
       prefrontal cortex
      </a>
      as a result of rewarding experiences such as food, sex, and
      <a href="/wiki/Neutral_stimulus" title="Neutral stimulus">
       neutral stimuli
      </a>
      that become
      <a href="/wiki/Classical_conditioning" title="Classical conditioning">
       associated
      </a>
      with them.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fn5_24-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-fn5-24">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        24
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The source of this dopamine is primarily the VTA, although the substantia nigra may also contribute. Electrical
      <a href="/wiki/Brain_stimulation_reward" title="Brain stimulation reward">
       stimulation of the VTA or its output pathways
      </a>
      can itself serve as a potent reward: animals will quickly learn to press a lever if it results in stimulation of dopamine release, and often will continue pressing the lever for a long time, at steadily increasing rates.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wise_25-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wise-25">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        25
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      A variety of drugs that increase dopamine levels are intrinsically rewarding and increase the effects of other types of reward.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wise_25-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wise-25">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        25
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In spite of the overwhelming evidence showing a strong association between dopamine and reward, there has been a great deal of dispute about whether the function of dopamine should be described as reward
      <i>
       per se
      </i>
      , or as some more complex construct that relates strongly to reward. The difficulty arises mainly from two observations: (1) in addition to being rewarding, dopamine is also arousing it produces a general increase in movement of all sorts; (2) dopamine release can be caused by events that do not seem to have anything to do with reward, most notably pain. One of the most popular alternatives to the reward theory is the "
      <a href="/wiki/Incentive_salience" title="Incentive salience">
       incentive salience
      </a>
      " theory, which argues that the function of dopamine is to increase the effects of motivators of all sorts, both positive and negative.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schultz_26-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Schultz-26">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        26
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      A substantial body of evidence suggests that dopamine encodes not reward itself, but rather
      <i>
       reward prediction error
      </i>
      , that is, the degree to which reward is surprising. According to this hypothesis, which derives initially from recordings made by
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Wolfram_Schultz&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Wolfram Schultz (page does not exist)">
       Wolfram Schultz
      </a>
      , rewards that are expected do not produce any activation of dopamine cells, but rewards that are greater than expected produce a short-lasting increase in dopamine, whereas the omission of an expected reward actually causes dopamine release to drop below its ordinary background level. The "prediction error" hypothesis has drawn particular interest from computational neuroscientists, because an influential computational-learning method known as
      <a href="/wiki/Temporal_difference_learning" title="Temporal difference learning">
       temporal difference learning
      </a>
      makes heavy use of a signal that encodes prediction error. This confluence of theory and data has led to a fertile interaction between theoretical and empirical neuroscientists.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schultz_26-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Schultz-26">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        26
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Recent research finds that while some dopaminergic neurons react in the way expected of reward neurons, others do not and seem to respond in regard to salience, including aversive stimuli.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Matsumoto_27-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Matsumoto-27">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        27
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      This research finds the reward neurons predominate in the ventromedial region of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pars_compacta" title="Pars compacta">
       substantia nigra pars compacta
      </a>
      , as well as in the
      <a href="/wiki/Ventral_tegmental_area" title="Ventral tegmental area">
       ventral tegmental area
      </a>
      . Neurons in these areas project mainly to the
      <a href="/wiki/Ventral_striatum" title="Ventral striatum">
       ventral striatum
      </a>
      and thus might transmit value-related information in regard to reward values.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Matsumoto_27-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Matsumoto-27">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        27
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The salience neurons are predominant in the dorsolateral area of the substantia nigra pars compacta which projects to the dorsal
      <a href="/wiki/Striatum" title="Striatum">
       striatum
      </a>
      and may relate to orienting behaviour.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Matsumoto_27-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Matsumoto-27">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        27
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      It has been suggested that the difference between these two types of dopaminergic neurons arises from their input: reward-linked ones have input from the
      <a href="/wiki/Basal_forebrain" title="Basal forebrain">
       basal forebrain
      </a>
      , while the salience-related ones from the
      <a href="/wiki/Habenula#Lateral_habenula" title="Habenula">
       lateral habenula
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Matsumoto_27-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-Matsumoto-27">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        27
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In primates, neurons from the regions of both the substantia nigra and VTA project to the prefrontal cortex;
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams_28-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Williams-28">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        28
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      the origins of the dopamine innervation of other cortical areas in primate have not been studied. It has been appreciated for many years that exposure to even mild, uncontrollable stress increases dopamine release in the rodent prefrontal cortex -e.g. reviewed in
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Deutch_29-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Deutch-29">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        29
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      - suggesting that dopamine salience cells have a large influence on this cortical region.
     </p>
     <h5>
      <span class="mw-headline" id=".22Seeking.22_versus_.22liking.22">
       "Seeking" versus "liking"
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Dopamine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title='Edit section: "Seeking" versus "liking"'>
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h5>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Kent_C._Berridge" title="Kent C. Berridge">
       Kent Berridge
      </a>
      and other researchers have argued for a distinction between reward, which is defined in terms of motivation, and pleasure, which is defined in terms of emotional expression. A simpler way of describing this is as a distinction between "seeking" and "liking". "Seeking" occurs when an animal, given access to some stimulus such as food, executes some type of active behavior in order to acquire it. "Liking" occurs when an animal shows expressions of happiness or satisfaction while consuming something. There is considerable evidence that the dopamine system is part of the brain system that mediates seeking but not part of the system that mediates liking. Drugs that increase the effects of dopamine (most notably stimulants such as methamphetamine or cocaine) produce corresponding increases in seeking behaviors, but do not greatly alter expressions of pleasure. Conversely, opiate drugs such as heroin or morphine produce increases in expressions of pleasure but do not greatly alter seeking behaviors. Animals in which the VTA dopamine system has been rendered inactive do not seek food, and will starve to death if left to themselves, but if food is placed in their mouths they will consume it and show facial expressions indicative of pleasure.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fn5_24-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-fn5-24">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        24
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Role_in_cognition">
       Role in cognition
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Dopamine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Role in cognition">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Dopamine's effects on higher cognitive function have been studied in monkeys and rodents. This work began with the landmark study of Brozoski et al., 1979 showing that depletion of catecholamines from the dorsolateral prefrontal cortex in monkeys impaired spatial working memory to the same degree as removing the cortex itself.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brozoski_30-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Brozoski-30">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        30
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      It is now known that both dopamine and norepinephrine have essential actions on prefrontal cortical function, and help coordinate cognitive state with arousal state.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Arnsten_31-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Arnsten-31">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        31
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Dopamine has an "inverted U" influence on prefrontal function through its actions on D1 receptors, where either too little or too much impairs working memory function.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vijayraghavan_32-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Vijayraghavan-32">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        32
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In the primate prefrontal cortex, dopamine D1 receptor stimulation selectively influences the firing of "Delay" cells (also called "Memory" cells), while dopamine D2 receptors selectively alter the firing of "Response cells".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wang_33-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wang-33">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        33
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Pharmacology">
       Pharmacology
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Dopamine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Pharmacology">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <table class="wikitable sortable" style="text-align:center; float:right">
      <caption>
       Binding profile of dopamine
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">
        <a href="#cite_note-34">
         <span>
          [
         </span>

         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </caption>
      <tr>
       <th>
        Macromolecule
       </th>

        <sub>

        </sub>

       </td>

         </sub>

       </td>
       <td>
        130
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dopamine_D2_receptor" title="Dopamine D2 receptor">

         <sub>

       </td>
      </tr>

       </td>

       </td>
      </tr>

         <sub>

         </sub>
        </a>
       </td>

       </td>
      </tr>

       </td>
      </tr>

         TAAR1
        </a>

       </td>
      </tr>
     </table>
     <p>
      Under the trade names
      <b>
       Intropin
      </b>
      ,
      <b>
       Dopastat
      </b>
      ,
      <b>
       Revimine
      </b>
      , or other names, dopamine can be used as a
      <a href="/wiki/Drug" title="Drug">
       drug
      </a>
      in
      <a href="/wiki/Injection_(medicine)" title="Injection (medicine)">
       injectable
      </a>
      form. It is most commonly used in the treatment of severe
      <a href="/wiki/Hypotension" title="Hypotension">
       hypotension
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Bradycardia" title="Bradycardia">
       bradycardia
      </a>
      (slow heart rate),
      <a href="/wiki/Shock_(circulatory)" title="Shock (circulatory)">
       circulatory shock
      </a>
      , or
      <a href="/wiki/Cardiac_arrest" title="Cardiac arrest">
       cardiac arrest
      </a>
      , especially in newborn infants. Its effects, depending on dosage, include an increase in sodium excretion by the kidneys, an increase in urine output, an increase in heart rate, and an increase in blood pressure. At a "cardiac/beta dose" of 5 to 10 g/kg/min, dopamine acts through the
      <a href="/wiki/Sympathetic_nervous_system" title="Sympathetic nervous system">
       sympathetic nervous system
      </a>
      to increase heart muscle contraction force and heart rate, thereby increasing cardiac output and blood pressure. At a "pressor/alpha dose" of 10 to 20 g/kg/min, dopamine also causes
      <a href="/wiki/Vasoconstriction" title="Vasoconstriction">
       vasoconstriction
      </a>
      that further increases blood pressure, but can produce negative side effects such as an impairment of kidney function and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cardiac_arrhythmias" title="Cardiac arrhythmias">
       cardiac arrhythmias
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BryantKnights2010_35-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-BryantKnights2010-35">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        35
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36">
       <a href="#cite_note-36">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        36
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Older literature also describes a so-called "renal/dopaminergic dose" of 2 to 5 g/kg/min thought to improve kidney function without other consequences, but recent reviews have concluded that doses at this low level are not clinically effective and may sometimes be harmful.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37">
       <a href="#cite_note-37">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        37
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Toxicity">
       Toxicity
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Dopamine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Toxicity">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The LD
      <sub>
       50
      </sub>
      , or dose which is expected to be lethal in 50% of the population, has been found to be: 59mg/kg (mouse; administered
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intravenously" title="Intravenously">
       intravenously
      </a>
      ); 950mg/kg (mouse; administered
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intraperitoneally" title="Intraperitoneally">
       intraperitoneally
      </a>
      ); 163mg/kg (rat; administered intraperitoneally); 79mg/kg (dog; administered intravenously).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
       <a href="#cite_note-38">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        38
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="L-DOPA">
       L-DOPA
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Dopamine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: L-DOPA">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/L-DOPA" title="L-DOPA">
       Levodopa
      </a>
      is a dopamine precursor used in various forms to treat
      <a href="/wiki/Parkinson%27s_disease" title="Parkinson's disease">
       Parkinson's disease
      </a>
      and dopa-responsive
      <a href="/wiki/Dystonia" title="Dystonia">
       dystonia
      </a>
      . It is typically co-administered with an inhibitor of peripheral decarboxylation (DDC,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aromatic-L-amino-acid_decarboxylase" title="Aromatic-L-amino-acid decarboxylase">
       dopa decarboxylase
      </a>
      ), such as
      <a href="/wiki/Carbidopa" title="Carbidopa">
       carbidopa
      </a>
      or
      <a href="/wiki/Benserazide" title="Benserazide">
       benserazide
      </a>
      . Inhibitors of alternative metabolic route for dopamine by
      <a href="/wiki/Catechol-O-methyl_transferase" title="Catechol-O-methyl transferase">
       catechol-O-methyl transferase
      </a>
      are also used. These include
      <a href="/wiki/Entacapone" title="Entacapone">
       entacapone
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Tolcapone" title="Tolcapone">
       tolcapone
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Psychostimulants">
       Psychostimulants
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Dopamine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Psychostimulants">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:542px;">
       <div style="clear: both; font-weight: bold; font-size: 106.4%; text-align: center; background-color: #EDC9AF;margin-bottom:3px">
        Pharmacodynamics of amphetamine enantiomers in a dopamine neuron
        <br/>
        <span class="noprint plainlinks navbar" style="white-space:nowrap;word-spacing:-.12em;">
         <a href="/wiki/Template:Amphetamine_pharmacodynamics" title="Template:Amphetamine pharmacodynamics">
          <span style="font-variant:small-caps;" title="View this template">
           v
          </span>
         </a>
         <b>
          
         </b>
         <a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Amphetamine_pharmacodynamics" title="Template talk:Amphetamine pharmacodynamics">
          <span style="font-variant:small-caps;" title="Discuss this template">
           t
          </span>
         </a>
         <b>
          
         </b>
         <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Amphetamine_pharmacodynamics&amp;action=edit">
          <span style="font-variant:small-caps;color:#002bb8;" title="Edit this template">
           e
          </span>
         </a>
        </span>
       </div>
       <div style="position:relative; width:540px; height:685px; overflow:hidden; border:solid #ccc 1px; background-color:white;">
        <div style="left:0px; top:0px; width:540px; position:absolute">
         <a href="//commons.wikimedia.org/wiki/File:TAAR1_Dopamine.svg" title="commons:File:TAAR1 Dopamine.svg">
          <img alt="A pharmacodynamic model of amphetamine and TAAR1" data-file-height="920" data-file-width="725" height="685" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/TAAR1_Dopamine.svg/540px-TAAR1_Dopamine.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/TAAR1_Dopamine.svg/810px-TAAR1_Dopamine.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/TAAR1_Dopamine.svg/1080px-TAAR1_Dopamine.svg.png 2x" width="540"/>
         </a>
        </div>
        <div style="text-align:center; font-size:13px; line-height:110%;">
         <div id="annotation_190x45" style="position:absolute; left:190px; top:45px; line-height:110%">
          <span style="background-color:transparent; color:black">
           <span style="background-color:transparent;">
            via
            <b>
             <a href="/wiki/Aromatic_L-amino_acid_decarboxylase" title="Aromatic L-amino acid decarboxylase">
              AADC
             </a>
            </b>
           </span>
          </span>
         </div>
        </div>
       </div>
       <div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">
        Amphetamine enters the presynaptic neuron across the neuronal membrane or through
        <abbr title="dopamine transporter">
         DAT
        </abbr>
        . Once inside, it binds to
        <abbr title="trace amine-associated receptor 1">
         TAAR1
        </abbr>
        or enters synaptic vesicles through
        <abbr title="vesicular monoamine transporter 2">
         VMAT2
        </abbr>
        . When amphetamine enters the synaptic vesicles through VMAT2, dopamine is released into the cytosol (yellow-orange area). When amphetamine binds to TAAR1, it reduces dopamine receptor firing rate via
        <a href="/wiki/G_protein-coupled_inwardly-rectifying_potassium_channel" title="G protein-coupled inwardly-rectifying potassium channel">
         potassium channels
        </a>
        and triggers
        <a href="/wiki/Protein_kinase_A" title="Protein kinase A">
         protein kinase A
        </a>
        (PKA) and
        <a href="/wiki/Protein_kinase_C" title="Protein kinase C">
         protein kinase C
        </a>
        (PKC) signaling, resulting in DAT phosphorylation.
        <span class="nowrap">
         PKA-phosphorylation
        </span>
        causes DAT to withdraw into the presynaptic neuron (
        <a href="/wiki/Endocytosis" title="Endocytosis">
         internalize
        </a>
        ) and cease transport.
        <span class="nowrap">
         PKC-phosphorylated
        </span>
        DAT may either operate in reverse or, like
        <span class="nowrap">
         PKA-phosphorylated
        </span>
        DAT, internalize and cease transport. Amphetamine is also known to increase intracellular calcium, a known effect of TAAR1 activation, which is associated with DAT phosphorylation through a
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ca2%2B/calmodulin-dependent_protein_kinase" title="Ca2+/calmodulin-dependent protein kinase">
         CAMK
        </a>
        -dependent pathway, in turn producing dopamine efflux.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Cocaine and
      <a href="/wiki/Substituted_amphetamine" title="Substituted amphetamine">
       substituted amphetamines
      </a>
      both increase dopamine neuronal activity; however, they do so by very different mechanisms. Cocaine is a
      <a href="/wiki/Dopamine_transporter" title="Dopamine transporter">
       dopamine transporter
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Norepinephrine_transporter" title="Norepinephrine transporter">
       norepinephrine transporter
      </a>
      blocker. It non-competitively inhibits dopamine reuptake, resulting in increased dopamine concentrations in the synaptic cleft. Like cocaine, substituted amphetamines increase the concentration of dopamine in the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Synaptic_cleft" title="Synaptic cleft">
       synaptic cleft
      </a>
      , but by a different, more complex mechanism (see image).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Miller_39-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Miller-39">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        39
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Antipsychotic_drugs">
       Antipsychotic drugs
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Dopamine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Antipsychotic drugs">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      A range of drugs that reduce dopamine activity have been found useful in the treatment of
      <a href="/wiki/Schizophrenia" title="Schizophrenia">
       schizophrenia
      </a>
      and other disorders that produce
      <a href="/wiki/Psychosis" title="Psychosis">
       psychosis
      </a>
      . These
      <a href="/wiki/Antipsychotic" title="Antipsychotic">
       antipsychotic
      </a>
      drugs are also sometimes known as neuroleptics or "major tranquilizers", in contrast to "minor tranquilizers" such as
      <a href="/wiki/Diazepam" title="Diazepam">
       Valium
      </a>
      that are used to treat anxiety or sleep disorders. These drugs have a broadly suppressive effect on most types of active behavior, and particularly reduce the delusional and agitated behavior characteristic of overt psychosis. The introduction of the first widely used antipsychotic drug,
      <a href="/wiki/Chlorpromazine" title="Chlorpromazine">
       chlorpromazine
      </a>
      (Thorazine), in the 1950s, led to the release of many schizophrenia patients from institutions in the years that followed.
     </p>
     <p>
      Even so, the widespread use of antipsychotic drugs has long been controversial. There are several reasons for this. First, these drugs are perceived as very aversive by people who have to take them, because they produce a general dullness of thought and suppress the ability to experience pleasure.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fn2_40-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-fn2-40">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        40
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Second, it is difficult to show that they act specifically against psychotic behaviors rather than merely suppressing all types of active behavior. Third, they can produce a range of serious side effects, including weight gain, diabetes, fatigue, sexual dysfunction, hormonal changes, and a type of movement disorder known as
      <a href="/wiki/Tardive_dyskinesia" title="Tardive dyskinesia">
       tardive dyskinesia
      </a>
      . Some of these side effects may continue long after the cessation of drug use, or even permanently.
     </p>
     <p>
      The first drugs introduced specifically for the treatment of psychosis all had strong direct effects on multiple aspects of dopamine function. Drugs of this type are known as "typical antipsychotics". Because of the problems they cause, there has been wide interest in newer types of drugs known as "atypical antipsychotics" or "second-generation antipsychotics", which aim to target the specific types of dopamine receptors involved in psychosis, and thereby reduce psychotic symptoms without producing as many undesirable side effects. There remains substantial dispute, however, about how much of an improvement in the patient experience these drugs produce.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Diseases_and_disorders">
       Diseases and disorders
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Dopamine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Diseases and disorders">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      The dopamine system plays a central role in a number of important medical conditions, including Parkinson's disease, attention deficit hyperactivity disorder, schizophrenia, and drug addiction.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Parkinson.27s_disease">
       Parkinson's disease
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Dopamine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Parkinson's disease">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Parkinson%27s_disease" title="Parkinson's disease">
       Parkinson's disease
      </a>
      is a disorder characterized by stiffness of the body, slowing of movement, and trembling of limbs when they are not in use. In advanced stages it progresses to dementia and eventually death. The main symptoms are caused by massive loss of dopamine-secreting cells in the
      <a href="/wiki/Substantia_nigra" title="Substantia nigra">
       substantia nigra
      </a>
      . These dopamine cells are especially vulnerable to damage, and a variety of insults, including
      <a href="/wiki/Encephalitis" title="Encephalitis">
       encephalitis
      </a>
      (as depicted in the book and movie "
      <a href="/wiki/Awakenings" title="Awakenings">
       Awakenings
      </a>
      "), repeated sports-related
      <a href="/wiki/Concussion" title="Concussion">
       concussions
      </a>
      , and some forms of chemical poisoning (ex.
      <a href="/wiki/MPTP" title="MPTP">
       MPTP
      </a>
      ), can lead to substantial cell loss, producing a
      <a href="/wiki/Parkinsonism" title="Parkinsonism">
       Parkinsonian syndrome
      </a>
      that is similar in its main features to Parkinson's disease. Most cases of Parkinson's disease, however, are "idiopathic", meaning that the cause of cell death cannot be identified.
     </p>
     <p>
      The most widely used treatment for Parkinsonism is administration of
      <a href="/wiki/L-DOPA" title="L-DOPA">
       L-DOPA
      </a>
      , the metabolic precursor for dopamine.
