This treatment cannot restore the dopamine cells that have been lost, but it causes the remaining cells to produce more dopamine, thereby compensating for the loss to at least some degree. In advanced stages the treatment begins to fail because the cell loss is so severe that the remaining ones cannot produce enough dopamine regardless of L-DOPA levels. As this stage is approached, the metabolic regulatory mechanisms in the dopamine cells, operating far above their normal level, become erratic, producing
      <a href="/wiki/Dopamine_dysregulation_syndrome" title="Dopamine dysregulation syndrome">
       dopamine dysregulation syndrome
      </a>
      , in which patients fluctuate unpredictably between states of hyperactivity and paralysis.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid17988927_41-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid17988927-41">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        41
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Attention_deficit_hyperactivity_disorder">
       Attention deficit hyperactivity disorder
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Dopamine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Attention deficit hyperactivity disorder">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Altered dopamine neurotransmission is implicated in
      <a href="/wiki/Attention_deficit_hyperactivity_disorder" title="Attention deficit hyperactivity disorder">
       attention deficit hyperactivity disorder
      </a>
      (ADHD), a condition associated with impaired ability to regulate attention, behavior, and/or impulses. There are some genetic links between dopamine receptors, the dopamine transporter and ADHD,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">
       <a href="#cite_note-42">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        42
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      in addition to links to other neurotransmitter receptors and transporters. The most important relationship between dopamine and ADHD involves the drugs that are used to treat ADHD. Some of the most effective therapeutic agents for ADHD are psychostimulants such as
      <a href="/wiki/Methylphenidate" title="Methylphenidate">
       methylphenidate
      </a>
      (Ritalin, Concerta) and
      <a href="/wiki/Amphetamine" title="Amphetamine">
       amphetamine
      </a>
      , drugs that increase both dopamine and norepinephrine levels in brain.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43">
       <a href="#cite_note-43">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        43
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Drug_addiction">
       Drug addiction
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Dopamine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Drug addiction">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%CE%94FosB" title="FosB">
       FosB
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      Current models of addiction from chronic drug use involve alterations in
      <a href="/wiki/Gene_expression" title="Gene expression">
       gene expression
      </a>
      in the nucleus accumbens, in turn affecting dopaminergic neurotransmission.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nestler.2C_Hyman.2C_and_Malenka_2_44-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Nestler.2C_Hyman.2C_and_Malenka_2-44">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        44
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nestler_45-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Nestler-45">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        45
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The most important
      <a href="/wiki/Transcription_factor" title="Transcription factor">
       transcription factors
      </a>
      that produce these alterations are
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%CE%94FosB" title="FosB">
       FosB
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CAMP_response_element_binding_protein" title="CAMP response element binding protein">
       cyclic adenosine monophosphate (cAMP) response element binding protein
      </a>
      (CREB), and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_factor_kappa_B" title="Nuclear factor kappa B">
       nuclear factor kappa B
      </a>
      (NFB).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nestler_45-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Nestler-45">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        45
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      FosB is the most significant among these, since its overexpression in the
      <a href="/wiki/Nucleus_accumbens" title="Nucleus accumbens">
       nucleus accumbens
      </a>
      is
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Necessary_and_sufficient" title="Necessary and sufficient">
       necessary and sufficient
      </a>
      for many of the neural adaptations seen in drug addiction;
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nestler_45-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Nestler-45">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        45
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      it has been implicated in addictions to many types of drugs, including
      <a href="/wiki/Cannabinoid" title="Cannabinoid">
       cannabinoids
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Cocaine" title="Cocaine">
       cocaine
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Nicotine" title="Nicotine">
       nicotine
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phenylcyclidine" title="Phenylcyclidine">
       phenylcyclidine
      </a>
      , and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Substituted_amphetamines" title="Substituted amphetamines">
       substituted amphetamines
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nestler.2C_Hyman.2C_and_Malenka_2_44-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Nestler.2C_Hyman.2C_and_Malenka_2-44">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        44
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nestler_45-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-Nestler-45">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        45
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%CE%94JunD" title="JunD">
       JunD
      </a>
      is the transcription factor which directly opposes FosB.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nestler_45-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-Nestler-45">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        45
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Increases in nucleus accumbens JunD expression can reduce or, with a large increase, even block most of the neural alterations seen in chronic drug abuse (i.e., the alterations mediated by FosB).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nestler_45-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-Nestler-45">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        45
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      FosB also plays an important role in regulating behavioral responses to natural rewards, such as palatable food, sex, and exercise.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nestler.2C_Hyman.2C_and_Malenka_2_44-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Nestler.2C_Hyman.2C_and_Malenka_2-44">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        44
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nestler_45-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-Nestler-45">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        45
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Since natural rewards, like drugs of abuse, induce FosB, chronic acquisition of these rewards can result in a similar pathological addictive state.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nestler.2C_Hyman.2C_and_Malenka_2_44-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-Nestler.2C_Hyman.2C_and_Malenka_2-44">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        44
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nestler_45-7">
       <a href="#cite_note-Nestler-45">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        45
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      FosB inhibitors (drugs that oppose its action) may be an effective treatment for addiction and addictive disorders.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Malenka_2009_04_46-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Malenka_2009_04-46">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        46
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Pain">
       Pain
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Dopamine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Pain">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Dopamine has been demonstrated to play a role in
      <a href="/wiki/Pain" title="Pain">
       pain
      </a>
      processing in multiple levels of the
      <a href="/wiki/Central_nervous_system" title="Central nervous system">
       central nervous system
      </a>
      including the
      <a href="/wiki/Spinal_cord" title="Spinal cord">
       spinal cord
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Periaqueductal_gray" title="Periaqueductal gray">
       periaqueductal gray
      </a>
      (PAG),
      <a href="/wiki/Thalamus" title="Thalamus">
       thalamus
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Basal_ganglia" title="Basal ganglia">
       basal ganglia
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Cingulate_cortex" title="Cingulate cortex">
       cingulate cortex
      </a>
      . Accordingly, decreased levels of dopamine have been associated with painful symptoms that frequently occur in
      <a href="/wiki/Parkinson%27s_disease" title="Parkinson's disease">
       Parkinson's disease
      </a>
      . Abnormalities in dopaminergic neurotransmission have also been demonstrated in painful clinical conditions, including
      <a href="/wiki/Burning_mouth_syndrome" title="Burning mouth syndrome">
       burning mouth syndrome
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid11207397_47-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid11207397-47">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        47
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Fibromyalgia" title="Fibromyalgia">
       fibromyalgia
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Restless_legs_syndrome" title="Restless legs syndrome">
       restless legs syndrome
      </a>
      . In general, the analgesic capacity of dopamine occurs as a result of dopamine D2 receptor activation; however, exceptions to this exist in the PAG, in which dopamine D1 receptor activation attenuates pain presumably
      <i>
       via
      </i>
      activation of neurons involved in descending inhibition.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid18457535_48-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid18457535-48">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        48
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In addition, D1 receptor activation in the insular cortex appears to attenuate subsequent pain-related behavior.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Nausea">
       Nausea
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Dopamine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Nausea">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Nausea and vomiting are largely determined by activity in a brainstem area known as the
      <a href="/wiki/Chemoreceptor_trigger_zone" title="Chemoreceptor trigger zone">
       chemoreceptor trigger zone
      </a>
      . This area contains a large population of type D2 dopamine receptors. Consequently, drugs that activate D2 receptors have a high potential to cause nausea. This group includes some medications that are administered for
      <a href="/wiki/Parkinson%27s_disease" title="Parkinson's disease">
       Parkinson's disease
      </a>
      , as well as other dopamine agonists such as
      <a href="/wiki/Apomorphine" title="Apomorphine">
       apomorphine
      </a>
      . In many cases, D2-receptor antagonists such as
      <a href="/wiki/Metoclopramide" title="Metoclopramide">
       metoclopramide
      </a>
      are useful as anti-nausea drugs.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Psychosis">
       Psychosis
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Dopamine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Psychosis">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Dopamine_hypothesis_of_schizophrenia" title="Dopamine hypothesis of schizophrenia">
       Dopamine hypothesis of schizophrenia
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      Abnormally high dopaminergic transmission has been linked to
      <a href="/wiki/Psychosis" title="Psychosis">
       psychosis
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Schizophrenia" title="Schizophrenia">
       schizophrenia
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49">
       <a href="#cite_note-49">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        49
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      However, clinical studies relating schizophrenia to brain dopamine metabolism have ranged from controversial to negative, with HVA levels in the CSF the same for schizophrenics and controls.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid9050120_50-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid9050120-50">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        50
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Increased dopaminergic functional activity, specifically in the
      <a href="/wiki/Mesolimbic_pathway" title="Mesolimbic pathway">
       mesolimbic pathway
      </a>
      , is found in schizophrenic individuals. However, decreased activity in another dopaminergic pathway, the
      <a href="/wiki/Mesocortical_pathway" title="Mesocortical pathway">
       mesocortical pathway
      </a>
      , may also be involved. The two pathways are thought to be responsible for differing sets of symptoms seen in schizophrenia.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2011)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Antipsychotic" title="Antipsychotic">
       Antipsychotic
      </a>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medication" title="Medication">
       medications
      </a>
      act largely as dopamine antagonists, inhibiting dopamine at the
      <a href="/wiki/Dopamine_receptor" title="Dopamine receptor">
       receptor
      </a>
      level, and thereby blocking the effects of the neurochemical in a dose-dependent manner. The older, so-called
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Typical_antipsychotics" title="Typical antipsychotics">
       typical antipsychotics
      </a>
      most commonly act on D2 receptors,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51">
       <a href="#cite_note-51">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        51
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      while the
      <a href="/wiki/Atypical_antipsychotic" title="Atypical antipsychotic">
       atypical drugs
      </a>
      also act on D1, D3 and D4 receptors, though they have a lower affinity for dopamine receptors in general.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid7475902_52-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid7475902-52">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        52
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The finding that drugs such as amphetamines, methamphetamine and cocaine, which can increase dopamine levels by more than tenfold,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53">
       <a href="#cite_note-53">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        53
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      can temporarily cause psychosis, provides further evidence for this link.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid2884687_54-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid2884687-54">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        54
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      However, many non-dopaminergic drugs can induce acute and chronic
      <a href="/wiki/Psychosis" title="Psychosis">
       psychosis
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cardinal_2011_diagnosis_psychosis_55-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Cardinal_2011_diagnosis_psychosis-55">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        55
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The NMDA antagonists Ketamine and PCP both are used in research to reproduce the positive and negative symptoms commonly associated with schizophrenia.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid10063482_56-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid10063482-56">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        56
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid9754841_57-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid9754841-57">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        57
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Dopaminergic dysregulation has also been linked to depressive disorders.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58">
       <a href="#cite_note-58">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        58
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Early research in humans used various methods of analyzing dopamine levels and function in depressed patients. Studies have reported that there is decreased concentration of
      <a href="/wiki/Tyrosine" title="Tyrosine">
       tyrosine
      </a>
      , a precursor to dopamine, in the blood plasma, ventricular spinal fluid, and lumbar spinal fluid of depressed patients compared to control subjects.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid5116991_59-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid5116991-59">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        59
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60">
       <a href="#cite_note-60">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        60
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      One study measured the amount of
      <a href="/wiki/Homovanillic_acid" title="Homovanillic acid">
       homovanillic acid
      </a>
      , the major metabolite of dopamine in the CSF, as a marker for the dopamine pathway turnover rate, and found decreased concentrations of homovanillic acid in the CSF of depressed patients.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid5796265_61-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid5796265-61">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        61
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Postmortem real time
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Reverse_transcriptase-polymerase_chain_reaction&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Reverse transcriptase-polymerase chain reaction (page does not exist)">
       reverse transcriptase-polymerase chain reaction
      </a>
      (RT-PCR) has also been used to find that gene expression of a specific subtype of dopamine receptor was elevated in the
      <a href="/wiki/Amygdala" title="Amygdala">
       amygdala
      </a>
      of people suffering from depression as compared to control subjects.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid18371940_62-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid18371940-62">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        62
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The action of commonly used antidepressant drugs also has yielded information about possible alterations of the dopaminergic pathway in treating depression. It has been reported that many antidepressant drugs increase extracellular dopamine concentrations in the rat prefrontal cortex,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid8866721_63-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid8866721-63">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        63
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      but vary greatly in their effects on the
      <a href="/wiki/Striatum" title="Striatum">
       striatum
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Nucleus_accumbens" title="Nucleus accumbens">
       nucleus accumbens
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid9888623_64-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid9888623-64">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        64
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid2901126_65-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid2901126-65">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        65
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      This can be compared to
      <a href="/wiki/Electroconvulsive_therapy" title="Electroconvulsive therapy">
       electro convulsive shock treatment
      </a>
      (ECT), which has been shown to have a multiple fold increase in striatal dopamine levels in rats.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid2482026_66-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid2482026-66">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        66
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      More recent research studies with rodents have found that depression-related behaviors are associated with dopaminergic system dysregulation.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tye_2012_537-541_67-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Tye_2012_537-541-67">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        67
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In rodents exposed to chronic mild stress, decreased escape behavior and decreased forced swimming is reversed with activation of the dopaminergic
      <a href="/wiki/Mesolimbic_pathway" title="Mesolimbic pathway">
       mesolimbic pathway
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tye_2012_537-541_67-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Tye_2012_537-541-67">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        67
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Also, rodents that are susceptible to depression-related behavior after
      <a href="/wiki/Social_defeat" title="Social defeat">
       social defeat
      </a>
      can have their behavior reversed with dopamine pathway activation.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid23235832_68-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid23235832-68">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        68
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Depletion of dopamine in the
      <a href="/wiki/Caudate_nucleus" title="Caudate nucleus">
       caudate nucleus
      </a>
      and nucleus accumbens has also been reported in cases of
      <a href="/wiki/Learned_helplessness" title="Learned helplessness">
       learned helplessness
      </a>
      in animals. These symptoms can be reversed with dopamine agonists and antidepressant administration prior to the learned helplessness protocol.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid2378927_69-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid2378927-69">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        69
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Comparative_biology_and_evolution">
       Comparative biology and evolution
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Dopamine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Comparative biology and evolution">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Microorganisms">
       Microorganisms
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Dopamine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Microorganisms">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      There are no reports of dopamine in
      <a href="/wiki/Archaea" title="Archaea">
       archaea
      </a>
      , but it has been detected in some types of
      <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">
       bacteria
      </a>
      and in a type of protozoan called
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Tetrahymena" title="Tetrahymena">
        Tetrahymena
       </a>
      </i>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70">
       <a href="#cite_note-70">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        70
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Perhaps more importantly, there are types of bacteria that contain homologs of all the enzymes that animals use to synthesize dopamine. It has even been proposed that animals derived their dopamine-synthesizing machinery from bacteria, via
      <a href="/wiki/Horizontal_gene_transfer" title="Horizontal gene transfer">
       horizontal gene transfer
      </a>
      that may have occurred relatively late in evolutionary time, perhaps as a result of the symbiotic incorporation of bacteria into
      <a href="/wiki/Eukaryote" title="Eukaryote">
       eukaryotic
      </a>
      cells that gave rise to
      <a href="/wiki/Mitochondrion" title="Mitochondrion">
       mitochondria
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71">
       <a href="#cite_note-71">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        71
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Animals">
       Animals
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Dopamine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Animals">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Dopamine is used as an intercellular messenger in virtually all multicellular animals. In sponges only a single report exists of the presence of dopamine, with no indication of its function;
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72">
       <a href="#cite_note-72">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        72
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      however, dopamine has been reported in the nervous systems of numerous radially symmetric species, including
      <a href="/wiki/Cnidaria" title="Cnidaria">
       cnidaria
      </a>
      (jellyfish, hydra, corals, etc.).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73">
       <a href="#cite_note-73">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        73
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      This dates the emergence of dopamine as a neurotransmitter back to the earliest appearance of the nervous system, over 500 million years ago in the
      <a href="/wiki/Cambrian" title="Cambrian">
       Cambrian era
      </a>
      . Among existing species, dopamine functions as a neurotransmitter in vertebrates,
      <a href="/wiki/Echinoderm" title="Echinoderm">
       echinoderms
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Arthropod" title="Arthropod">
       arthropods
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Mollusca" title="Mollusca">
       molluscs
      </a>
      , and several types of worms.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74">
       <a href="#cite_note-74">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        74
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75">
       <a href="#cite_note-75">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        75
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In every type of animal that has been examined, dopamine acts to modify motor behavior.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barron_76-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Barron-76">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        76
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In the much-studied nematode worm
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Caenorhabditis_elegans" title="Caenorhabditis elegans">
        Caenorhabditis elegans
       </a>
      </i>
      , it reduces locomotion and increases food-exploratory movements; in
      <a href="/wiki/Planarian" title="Planarian">
       planarian
      </a>
      worms it produces "screw-like" movements; in
      <a href="/wiki/Leech" title="Leech">
       leeches
      </a>
      it inhibits swimming and promotes crawling; etc. Across a wide range of vertebrates, dopamine has an "activating" effect on behavior-switching and response selection, comparable to its effect in mammals.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barron_76-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Barron-76">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        76
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Dopamine also consistently plays a role in reward learning, in all animal groups that have been examined except arthropods, although recent evidence suggests that dopamine at least mediates reward learning in fruit flies.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tanimoto_77-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Tanimoto-77">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        77
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Waddell_78-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Waddell-78">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        78
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In nematodes, planarians, molluscs, D. melanogaster, and vertebrates, animals can be trained to repeat an action if it is consistently followed by an increase in dopamine levels.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barron_76-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Barron-76">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        76
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Arthropods have long been believed to be an exception, though. In these species insects, crustaceans, etc. dopamine was thought to have an aversive effect, with reward instead mediated by
      <a href="/wiki/Octopamine" title="Octopamine">
       octopamine
      </a>
      , a neurotransmitter that is not found in vertebrates but is thought to be closely related to
      <a href="/wiki/Norepinephrine" title="Norepinephrine">
       norepinephrine
      </a>
      . A number of studies reveal that the appetitive effect of octopamine comes from its activation of a set of dopaminergic neurons which had not been accessed in previous efforts.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tanimoto_77-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Tanimoto-77">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        77
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Waddell_78-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Waddell-78">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        78
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      A separate dopamine-producing population of cells appears to increase aversion learning of olfactory cues, much like in mammals.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WaddellReview_79-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-WaddellReview-79">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        79
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Plants">
       Plants
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Dopamine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Plants">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bananas_white_background_DS.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="3000" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Bananas_white_background_DS.jpg/220px-Bananas_white_background_DS.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Bananas_white_background_DS.jpg/330px-Bananas_white_background_DS.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Bananas_white_background_DS.jpg/440px-Bananas_white_background_DS.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bananas_white_background_DS.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Dopamine can be found in the
        <a href="/wiki/Banana_peel" title="Banana peel">
         peel
        </a>
        and fruit pulp of
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bananas" title="Bananas">
         bananas
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Many plants synthesize dopamine to varying degrees, including a variety of food plants. The highest concentrations have been observed in bananas the fruit pulp of red and yellow bananas contains dopamine at levels of 40 to 50 parts per million by weight. Potatoes, avocados, broccoli, and
      <a href="/wiki/Brussels_sprout" title="Brussels sprout">
       Brussels sprouts
      </a>
      may also contain dopamine at levels of 1 part per million or more; oranges, tomatoes, spinach, beans, and other plants contain measurable concentrations less than 1 part per million.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kulma_80-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Kulma-80">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        80
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The dopamine in plants is synthesized from the amino acid tyrosine, by biochemical mechanisms similar to those that animals use. It can be metabolized in a number of ways, producing
      <a href="/wiki/Melanin" title="Melanin">
       melanin
      </a>
      and a variety of alkaloids as byproducts.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kulma_80-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Kulma-80">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        80
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The functions of plant catecholamines have not been clearly established, but there is evidence that they play a role in the response to stressors such as bacterial infection, act as growth-promoting factors in some situations, and modify the way that sugars are metabolized. The receptors that mediate these actions have not yet been identified, nor have the intracellular mechanisms that they activate.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kulma_80-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Kulma-80">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        80
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Dopamine consumed in food cannot act on the brain, because it cannot cross the
      <a href="/wiki/Blood%E2%80%93brain_barrier" title="Bloodbrain barrier">
       bloodbrain barrier
      </a>
      . However, there are also a variety of plants that contain
      <a href="/wiki/L-DOPA" title="L-DOPA">
       L-DOPA
      </a>
      , the metabolic precursor of dopamine.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ingle_81-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Ingle-81">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        81
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The highest concentrations are found in the leaves and bean pods of plants of the genus
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Mucuna" title="Mucuna">
        Mucuna
       </a>
      </i>
      , especially in
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Mucuna_pruriens" title="Mucuna pruriens">
        Mucuna pruriens
       </a>
      </i>
      (velvet beans), which have been used as a source for L-DOPA as a drug.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82">
       <a href="#cite_note-82">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        82
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Another plant containing substantial amounts of L-DOPA is
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Vicia_faba" title="Vicia faba">
        Vicia faba
       </a>
      </i>
      , the plant that produces fava beans (also known as "broad beans"). The level of L-DOPA in the beans, however, is much lower than in the pod shells and other parts of the plant.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83">
       <a href="#cite_note-83">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        83
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The seeds of
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cassia_(legume)" title="Cassia (legume)">
        Cassia
       </a>
      </i>
      and
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Bauhinia" title="Bauhinia">
        Bauhinia
       </a>
      </i>
      trees also contain substantial amounts of L-DOPA.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ingle_81-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Ingle-81">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        81
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In the marine green alga
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Ulvaria_obscura" title="Ulvaria obscura">
        Ulvaria obscura
       </a>
      </i>
      , which is a major component of some
      <a href="/wiki/Algal_bloom" title="Algal bloom">
       algal blooms
      </a>
      , dopamine is present in very high concentrations, estimated at 4.4% of dry weight. There is evidence that this dopamine functions as an anti-
      <a href="/wiki/Herbivore" title="Herbivore">
       herbivore
      </a>
      defense, reducing consumption by snails and
      <a href="/wiki/Isopoda" title="Isopoda">
       isopods
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid16489461_84-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid16489461-84">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        84
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="As_a_precursor_for_melanin">
       As a precursor for melanin
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Dopamine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: As a precursor for melanin">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Melanin" title="Melanin">
       Melanins
      </a>
      are a family of dark-pigmented substances that are found in a wide range of organisms. Their physical properties make them difficult to work with experimentally, and consequently a number of aspects of their biochemistry are not well understood. Chemically they are closely related to dopamine, and there is a type of melanin, known as "dopamine-melanin", that can be synthesized by oxidation of dopamine via the enzyme
      <a href="/wiki/Tyrosinase" title="Tyrosinase">
       tyrosinase
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85">
       <a href="#cite_note-85">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        85
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The melanin that darkens human skin is not of this type: it is synthesized by a pathway that uses
      <a href="/wiki/L-DOPA" title="L-DOPA">
       L-DOPA
      </a>
      as a precursor but not dopamine. However, there is substantial evidence that the "neuromelanin" that gives a dark color to the brain's
      <a href="/wiki/Substantia_nigra" title="Substantia nigra">
       substantia nigra
      </a>
      is at least in part dopamine-melanin.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86">
       <a href="#cite_note-86">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        86
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Dopamine-derived melanin probably appears in at least some other biological systems as well. Some of the dopamine in plants is likely to be used as a precursor for dopamine-melanin.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87">
       <a href="#cite_note-87">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        87
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The complex patterns that appear on butterfly wings, as well as black-and-white stripes on the bodies of insect larvae, are also thought to be caused by spatially structured accumulations of dopamine-melanin.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88">
       <a href="#cite_note-88">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        88
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Biochemical_mechanisms">
       Biochemical mechanisms
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Dopamine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Biochemical mechanisms">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      Structurally, dopamine belongs to the
      <a href="/wiki/Catecholamine" title="Catecholamine">
       catecholamine
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Phenethylamine" title="Phenethylamine">
       phenethylamine
      </a>
      classes. In biological systems, dopamine is synthesized in brain cells and adrenal cells from the precursor
      <a href="/wiki/L-DOPA" title="L-DOPA">
       L-DOPA
      </a>
      . In brain cells, it is transported to
      <a href="/wiki/Chemical_synapse" title="Chemical synapse">
       synaptic
      </a>
      sites and packaged into vesicles for release, which occurs during synaptic transmission. After release, free dopamine is either reabsorbed into the presynaptic terminal for reuse, or broken down by the enzymes
      <a href="/wiki/Monoamine_oxidase" title="Monoamine oxidase">
       monoamine oxidase
      </a>
      or COMT, producing a variety of degradation metabolites.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Biosynthesis">
       Biosynthesis
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Dopamine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Biosynthesis">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:592px;">
       <div style="clear: both; font-weight: bold; font-size: 106.4%; text-align: center; background-color: transparent;margin-bottom:3px">
        Human biosynthesis pathway for trace amines and catecholamines
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Trace_amine_template_1_89-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-Trace_amine_template_1-89">
          <span>
           [
          </span>
          89
          <span>
           ]
          </span>
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Trace_amine_template_2_90-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-Trace_amine_template_2-90">
          <span>
           [
          </span>
          90
          <span>
           ]
          </span>
         </a>
        </sup>
       </div>
       <div style="position:relative; width:590px; height:585px; overflow:hidden; border:solid #ccc 1px; background-color:white;">
        <div style="left:5px; top:0px; width:580px; position:absolute">
         <a href="//commons.wikimedia.org/wiki/File:Catecholamine_and_trace_amine_biosynthesis.png" title="commons:File:Catecholamine and trace amine biosynthesis.png">
          <img alt="Graphic of trace amine and catecholamine metabolism" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="585" height="595" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Catecholamine_and_trace_amine_biosynthesis.png/580px-Catecholamine_and_trace_amine_biosynthesis.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/6a/Catecholamine_and_trace_amine_biosynthesis.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/6a/Catecholamine_and_trace_amine_biosynthesis.png 2x" width="580"/>
         </a>
        </div>
        <div style="text-align:center; font-size:14px; line-height:110%;">
         <div id="annotation_40x60" style="position:absolute; left:40px; top:60px; line-height:110%">
          <span style="background-color:transparent; color:black">
           <span style="background-color:transparent;">
            <a href="/wiki/Phenylalanine" title="Phenylalanine">
             L-Phenylalanine
            </a>
           </span>
          </span>
         </div>
         <div id="annotation_50x250" style="position:absolute; left:50px; top:250px; line-height:110%">
          <span style="background-color:transparent;">
           <a href="/wiki/Tyrosine" title="Tyrosine">
            L-Tyrosine
           </a>
          </span>
         </div>
         <div id="annotation_60x410" style="position:absolute; left:60px; top:410px; line-height:110%">
          <span style="background-color:transparent;">
           <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/L-Dopa" title="L-Dopa">
            L-Dopa
           </a>
          </span>
         </div>
         <div id="annotation_50x565" style="position:absolute; left:50px; top:565px; line-height:110%">
          <span style="background-color:transparent;">
           <a href="/wiki/Epinephrine" title="Epinephrine">
            Epinephrine
           </a>
          </span>
         </div>
         <div id="annotation_245x60" style="position:absolute; left:245px; top:60px; line-height:110%">
          <span style="background-color:transparent;">
           <a href="/wiki/Phenethylamine" title="Phenethylamine">
            Phenethylamine
           </a>
          </span>
         </div>
         <div id="annotation_245x250" style="position:absolute; left:245px; top:250px; line-height:110%">
          <span style="background-color:transparent;">
           <a href="/wiki/Tyramine" title="Tyramine">
            <i>
             p
            </i>
            -Tyramine
           </a>
          </span>
         </div>
         <div id="annotation_245x410" style="position:absolute; left:245px; top:410px; line-height:110%">
          <span style="background-color:transparent;">
           <strong class="selflink">
            Dopamine
           </strong>
          </span>
         </div>
         <div id="annotation_245x565" style="position:absolute; left:245px; top:565px; line-height:110%">
          <span style="background-color:transparent;">
           <a href="/wiki/Norepinephrine" title="Norepinephrine">
            Norepinephrine
           </a>
          </span>
         </div>
         <div id="annotation_405x60" style="position:absolute; left:405px; top:60px; line-height:110%">
          <span style="background-color:transparent;">
           <a href="/wiki/N-Methylphenethylamine" title="N-Methylphenethylamine">
            <i>
             N
            </i>
            -Methylphenethylamine
           </a>
          </span>
         </div>
         <div id="annotation_440x150" style="position:absolute; left:440px; top:150px; line-height:110%">
          <span style="background-color:transparent;">
           <a href="/wiki/N-Methyltyramine" title="N-Methyltyramine">
            <i>
             N
            </i>
            -Methyltyramine
           </a>
          </span>
         </div>
         <div id="annotation_440x295" style="position:absolute; left:440px; top:295px; line-height:110%">
          <span style="background-color:transparent;">
           <a href="/wiki/Octopamine" title="Octopamine">
            <i>
             p
            </i>
            -Octopamine
           </a>
          </span>
         </div>
         <div id="annotation_445x445" style="position:absolute; left:445px; top:445px; line-height:110%">
          <span style="background-color:transparent;">
           <a href="/wiki/Synephrine" title="Synephrine">
            Synephrine
           </a>
          </span>
         </div>
         <div id="annotation_425x565" style="position:absolute; left:425px; top:565px; line-height:110%">
          <span style="background-color:transparent;">
           <a href="/wiki/3-Methoxytyramine" title="3-Methoxytyramine">
            3-Methoxytyramine
           </a>
          </span>
         </div>
         <div id="annotation_170x16" style="position:absolute; left:170px; top:16px; line-height:110%">
          <span style="background-color:transparent;">
           <a href="/wiki/Aromatic_L-amino_acid_decarboxylase" title="Aromatic L-amino acid decarboxylase">
            AADC
           </a>
          </span>
         </div>
         <div id="annotation_170x203" style="position:absolute; left:170px; top:203px; line-height:110%">
          <span style="background-color:transparent;">
           <a href="/wiki/Aromatic_L-amino_acid_decarboxylase" title="Aromatic L-amino acid decarboxylase">
            AADC
           </a>
          </span>
         </div>
         <div id="annotation_170x368" style="position:absolute; left:170px; top:368px; line-height:110%">
          <span style="background-color:transparent;">
           <a href="/wiki/Aromatic_L-amino_acid_decarboxylase" title="Aromatic L-amino acid decarboxylase">
            AADC
           </a>
          </span>
         </div>
         <div id="annotation_425x345" style="position:absolute; left:425px; top:345px; line-height:110%">
          <span style="background-color:transparent;">
           <a href="/wiki/Phenylethanolamine_N-methyltransferase" title="Phenylethanolamine N-methyltransferase">
            PNMT
           </a>
          </span>
         </div>
         <div id="annotation_380x17" style="position:absolute; left:380px; top:17px; line-height:110%">
          <span style="background-color:transparent;">
           <a href="/wiki/Phenylethanolamine_N-methyltransferase" title="Phenylethanolamine N-methyltransferase">
            PNMT
           </a>
          </span>
         </div>
         <div id="annotation_165x527" style="position:absolute; left:165px; top:527px; line-height:110%">
          <span style="background-color:transparent;">
           <a href="/wiki/Phenylethanolamine_N-methyltransferase" title="Phenylethanolamine N-methyltransferase">
            PNMT
           </a>
          </span>
         </div>
         <div id="annotation_359x170" style="position:absolute; left:359px; top:170px; line-height:110%">
          <span style="background-color:transparent;">
           <a href="/wiki/Phenylethanolamine_N-methyltransferase" title="Phenylethanolamine N-methyltransferase">
            PNMT
           </a>
          </span>
         </div>
         <div id="annotation_55x130" style="position:absolute; left:55px; top:130px; line-height:110%">
          <span style="background-color:transparent;">
           <a href="/wiki/Biopterin-dependent_aromatic_amino_acid_hydroxylase" title="Biopterin-dependent aromatic amino acid hydroxylase">
            AAAH
           </a>
          </span>
         </div>
         <div id="annotation_55x305" style="position:absolute; left:55px; top:305px; line-height:110%">
          <span style="background-color:transparent;">
           <a href="/wiki/Biopterin-dependent_aromatic_amino_acid_hydroxylase" title="Biopterin-dependent aromatic amino acid hydroxylase">
            AAAH
           </a>
          </span>
         </div>
         <div id="annotation_325x460" style="position:absolute; left:325px; top:460px; line-height:110%">
          <span style="background-color:transparent;">
           <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catechol_O-methyltransferase" title="Catechol O-methyltransferase">
            COMT
           </a>
          </span>
         </div>
         <div id="annotation_235x460" style="position:absolute; left:235px; top:460px; line-height:110%">
          <span style="background-color:transparent;">
           <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dopamine_beta_hydroxylase" title="Dopamine beta hydroxylase">
            DBH
           </a>
          </span>
         </div>
         <div id="annotation_363x250" style="position:absolute; left:363px; top:250px; line-height:110%">
          <span style="background-color:transparent;">
           <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dopamine_beta_hydroxylase" title="Dopamine beta hydroxylase">
            DBH
           </a>
          </span>
         </div>
        </div>
       </div>
       <div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">
        This is the biosynthesis of catecholamines, including dopamine, as well as phenethylaminergic trace amines from the amino acid phenylalanine. Abbreviations used:
        <br/>
        <span class="nowrap">
         DBH: Dopamine -hydroxylase
        </span>
        <br/>
        <span class="nowrap">
         AADC: Aromatic L-amino acid decarboxylase
        </span>
        <br/>
        <span class="nowrap">
         AAAH: (Biopterin-dependent) aromatic amino acid hydroxylase
        </span>
        <br/>
        <span class="nowrap">
         COMT: Catechol O-methyltransferase
        </span>
        <br/>
        <span class="nowrap">
         PNMT: Phenylethanolamine N-methyltransferase
        </span>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Dopamine is
      <a href="/wiki/Biosynthesis" title="Biosynthesis">
       synthesized
      </a>
      in a restricted set of cell types, mainly
      <a href="/wiki/Neuron" title="Neuron">
       neurons
      </a>
      and cells in the
      <a href="/wiki/Adrenal_medulla" title="Adrenal medulla">
       medulla
      </a>
      of the
      <a href="/wiki/Adrenal_gland" title="Adrenal gland">
       adrenal glands
      </a>
      . This is the
      <a href="/wiki/Metabolic_pathway" title="Metabolic pathway">
       metabolic pathway
      </a>
      :
     </p>
     <ul>
      <li>
       <small>
        L
       </small>
       -Phenylalanine 
       <small>
        L
       </small>
       -Tyrosine 
       <small>
        L
       </small>
       -DOPA  Dopamine
      </li>
     </ul>
     <p>
      Thus the direct precursor of dopamine is
      <a href="/wiki/L-DOPA" title="L-DOPA">
       L-DOPA
      </a>
      , but this itself can be synthesized from the essential amino acid
      <a href="/wiki/Phenylalanine" title="Phenylalanine">
       phenylalanine
      </a>
      or the non-essential amino acid
      <a href="/wiki/Tyrosine" title="Tyrosine">
       tyrosine
      </a>
      . These amino acids are found in nearly every protein and as such are provided from ingestion of protein-containing food, with tyrosine being the most common. Although dopamine itself is also found in many types of food, it is incapable of crossing the
      <a href="/wiki/Blood%E2%80%93brain_barrier" title="Bloodbrain barrier">
       bloodbrain barrier
      </a>
      that surrounds and protects the brain. It must therefore be synthesized inside the brain in order to perform its neural actions.
     </p>
     <p>
      <small>
       L
      </small>
      -Phenylalanine is converted into
      <small>
       L
      </small>
      -tyrosine by the enzyme
      <a href="/wiki/Phenylalanine_hydroxylase" title="Phenylalanine hydroxylase">
       phenylalanine hydroxylase
      </a>
      (PAH), with molecular
      <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">
       oxygen
      </a>
      (O
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      ) and
      <a href="/wiki/Tetrahydrobiopterin" title="Tetrahydrobiopterin">
       tetrahydrobiopterin
      </a>
      (THB) as
      <a href="/wiki/Cofactor_(biochemistry)" title="Cofactor (biochemistry)">
       cofactors
      </a>
      .
      <small>
       L
      </small>
      -Tyrosine is converted into
      <small>
       L
      </small>
      -DOPA by the enzyme
      <a href="/wiki/Tyrosine_hydroxylase" title="Tyrosine hydroxylase">
       tyrosine hydroxylase
      </a>
      (TH), with
      <a href="/wiki/Tetrahydrobiopterin" title="Tetrahydrobiopterin">
       tetrahydrobiopterin
      </a>
      (THB), O
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Ferrous" title="Ferrous">
       ferrous
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">
       iron
      </a>
      (Fe
      <sup>
       2+
      </sup>
      ) as cofactors.
      <small>
       L
      </small>
      -DOPA is converted into dopamine by the enzyme
      <a href="/wiki/Aromatic_L-amino_acid_decarboxylase" title="Aromatic L-amino acid decarboxylase">
       aromatic
       <small>
        L
       </small>
       -amino acid decarboxylase
      </a>
      (AADC; also known as DOPA decarboxylase (DDC)), with
      <a href="/wiki/Pyridoxal_phosphate" title="Pyridoxal phosphate">
       pyridoxal phosphate
      </a>
      (PLP) as the cofactor.
     </p>
     <p>
      Dopamine itself is also used as precursor in the synthesis of the neurotransmitters
      <a href="/wiki/Norepinephrine" title="Norepinephrine">
       norepinephrine
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Epinephrine" title="Epinephrine">
       epinephrine
      </a>
      . Dopamine is converted into norepinephrine by the enzyme
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dopamine_beta_hydroxylase" title="Dopamine beta hydroxylase">
       dopamine -hydroxylase
      </a>
      (DBH), with O
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Ascorbic_acid" title="Ascorbic acid">
       <small>
        L
       </small>
       -ascorbic acid
      </a>
      as cofactors. Norepinephrine is converted into epinephrine by the enzyme
      <a href="/wiki/Phenylethanolamine_N-methyltransferase" title="Phenylethanolamine N-methyltransferase">
       phenylethanolamine
       <i>
        N
       </i>
       -methyltransferase
      </a>
      (PNMT) with
      <a href="/wiki/S-Adenosyl_methionine" title="S-Adenosyl methionine">
       <i>
        S
       </i>
       -adenosyl-
       <small>
        L
       </small>
       -methionine
      </a>
      (SAM) as the cofactor.
     </p>
     <p>
      It should be noted that some of the cofactors also require their own synthesis. Deficiency in any required amino acid or cofactor will result in subsequent dopamine, norepinephrine, and epinephrine biosynthesis impairment and deficiency.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Storage.2C_release.2C_and_reuptake">
       Storage, release, and reuptake
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Dopamine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Storage, release, and reuptake">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote">
      Further information:
      <a href="/wiki/Dopamine#Psychostimulants" title="Dopamine">
       Dopamine  Psychostimulants
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      Inside the brain dopamine functions as a
      <a href="/wiki/Neurotransmitter" title="Neurotransmitter">
       neurotransmitter
      </a>
      , and is controlled by a set of mechanisms that are common to monoamine neurotransmitters. After synthesis, dopamine is transported from the
      <a href="/wiki/Cytosol" title="Cytosol">
       cytosol
      </a>
      into
      <a href="/wiki/Vesicle_(biology_and_chemistry)" title="Vesicle (biology and chemistry)">
       synaptic vesicles
      </a>
      by the
      <a href="/wiki/Vesicular_monoamine_transporter_2" title="Vesicular monoamine transporter 2">
       vesicular monoamine transporter 2
      </a>
      (VMAT2). Dopamine is stored in these vesicles until it is ejected into the
      <a href="/wiki/Chemical_synapse" title="Chemical synapse">
       synaptic cleft
      </a>
      by one of two mechanisms: typically, an
      <a href="/wiki/Action_potential" title="Action potential">
       action potential
      </a>
      occurs and causes the vesicles to release their contents directly into the
      <a href="/wiki/Chemical_synapse" title="Chemical synapse">
       synaptic cleft
      </a>
      through a process called
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Exocytosis" title="Exocytosis">
        exocytosis
       </a>
      </i>
      ;
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Miller_39-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Miller-39">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        39
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      however, dopamine neurons which are
      <a class="extiw" href="//en.wiktionary.org/wiki/colocalize" title="wikt:colocalize">
       colocalized
      </a>
      with
      <a href="/wiki/TAAR1" title="TAAR1">
       TAAR1
      </a>
      can release dopamine into the synapse in a manner identical to
      <a href="/wiki/Amphetamine" title="Amphetamine">
       amphetamine
      </a>
      in the presence of sufficient concentrations of endogenous
      <a href="/wiki/Phenethylamine" title="Phenethylamine">
       phenethylamine
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Miller_39-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Miller-39">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        39
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Once in the synapse, dopamine binds to and activates
      <a href="/wiki/Dopamine_receptor" title="Dopamine receptor">
       dopamine receptors
      </a>
      , which can be located either on
      <a href="/wiki/Chemical_synapse" title="Chemical synapse">
       postsynaptic
      </a>
      target cells or on the membrane of the presynaptic dopamine-releasing cell itself (i.e.,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/D2sh" title="D2sh">
       D2 short
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Autoreceptor" title="Autoreceptor">
       autoreceptors
      </a>
      ).
     </p>
     <p>
      After an action potential, the dopamine molecules quickly become unbound from their receptors. They are then absorbed back into the presynaptic cell, via
      <a href="/wiki/Reuptake" title="Reuptake">
       reuptake
      </a>
      mediated either by the high-affinity
      <a href="/wiki/Dopamine_transporter" title="Dopamine transporter">
       dopamine transporter
      </a>
      (DAT) or by the low-affinity
      <a href="/wiki/Plasma_membrane_monoamine_transporter" title="Plasma membrane monoamine transporter">
       plasma membrane monoamine transporter
      </a>
      (PMAT). Once back in the cytosol, dopamine is subsequently repackaged into vesicles by VMAT2, making it available for future release.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Degradation">
       Degradation
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Dopamine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Degradation">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dopamine_degradation.svg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="809" data-file-width="844" height="383" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Dopamine_degradation.svg/400px-Dopamine_degradation.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Dopamine_degradation.svg/600px-Dopamine_degradation.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Dopamine_degradation.svg/800px-Dopamine_degradation.svg.png 2x" width="400"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dopamine_degradation.svg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Dopamine degradation
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Dopamine is broken down into inactive metabolites by a set of enzymes,
      <a href="/wiki/Monoamine_oxidase" title="Monoamine oxidase">
       monoamine oxidase
      </a>
      (MAO),
      <a href="/wiki/Aldehyde_dehydrogenase" title="Aldehyde dehydrogenase">
       aldehyde dehydrogenase
      </a>
      (ALDH), and
      <a href="/wiki/Catechol-O-methyl_transferase" title="Catechol-O-methyl transferase">
       catechol-
       <i>
        O
       </i>
       -methyl transferase
      </a>
      (COMT), acting in sequence. Both
      <a href="/wiki/Protein_isoform" title="Protein isoform">
       isoforms
      </a>
      of MAO,
      <a href="/wiki/Monoamine_oxidase_A" title="Monoamine oxidase A">
       MAO-A
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Monoamine_oxidase_B" title="Monoamine oxidase B">
       MAO-B
      </a>
      , are equally effective.
     </p>
     <p>
      The metabolites produced by these processes are:
     </p>
     <ul>
      <li>
       DOPAL (
       <a href="/wiki/3,4-Dihydroxyphenylacetaldehyde" title="3,4-Dihydroxyphenylacetaldehyde">
        3,4-Dihydroxyphenylacetaldehyde
       </a>
       )
      </li>
      <li>
       DOPAC (
       <a href="/wiki/3,4-Dihydroxyphenylacetic_acid" title="3,4-Dihydroxyphenylacetic acid">
        3,4-Dihydroxyphenylacetic acid
       </a>
       )
      </li>
      <li>
       DOPET (3,4-dihydroxyphenylethanol, also known as
       <a href="/wiki/Hydroxytyrosol" title="Hydroxytyrosol">
        Hydroxytyrosol
       </a>
       )
      </li>
      <li>
       MOPET (3-methoxy-4-hydroxyphenylethanol, also known as
       <a href="/wiki/Homovanillyl_alcohol" title="Homovanillyl alcohol">
        Homovanillyl alcohol
       </a>
       )
      </li>
      <li>
       3-MT (
       <a href="/wiki/3-Methoxytyramine" title="3-Methoxytyramine">
        3-Methoxytyramine
       </a>
       , a partial
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/TAAR1_agonist" title="TAAR1 agonist">
        TAAR1 agonist
       </a>
       )
      </li>
      <li>
       HVA (
       <a href="/wiki/Homovanillic_acid" title="Homovanillic acid">
        Homovanillic acid
       </a>
       )
      </li>
     </ul>
     <p>
      All of these are intermediate metabolites except MOPET and HVA, which are filtered from the bloodstream by the kidneys and then excreted in the urine.
     </p>
     <p>
      The specific reactions that make up these pathways are:
     </p>
     <ul>
      <li>
       Dopamine  DOPAL, mediated by MAO
      </li>
      <li>
       DOPAL  DOPAC, mediated by
       <a href="/wiki/Aldehyde_dehydrogenase" title="Aldehyde dehydrogenase">
        ALDH
       </a>
      </li>
      <li>
       DOPAL  DOPET, mediated by
       <a href="/wiki/Aldose_reductase" title="Aldose reductase">
        aldose reductase
       </a>
       (minor pathway)
      </li>
      <li>
       DOPAC  HVA, mediated by COMT
      </li>
      <li>
       DOPET  MOPET, mediated by COMT
      </li>
      <li>
       Dopamine  3-MT, mediated by COMT
      </li>
      <li>
       3-MT  HVA, mediated by MAO
      </li>
     </ul>
     <p>
      In most areas of the brain, including the
      <a href="/wiki/Striatum" title="Striatum">
       striatum
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Basal_ganglia" title="Basal ganglia">
       basal ganglia
      </a>
      , dopamine is inactivated by reuptake via the DAT, then enzymatic breakdown by MAO into DOPAC. In the
      <a href="/wiki/Prefrontal_cortex" title="Prefrontal cortex">
       prefrontal cortex
      </a>
      , however, there are very few DAT proteins, and dopamine is inactivated instead by reuptake via the
      <a href="/wiki/Norepinephrine_transporter" title="Norepinephrine transporter">
       norepinephrine transporter
      </a>
      (NET), presumably on neighboring norepinephrine neurons, then enzymatic breakdown by COMT into 3-MT.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid11784783_91-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid11784783-91">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        91
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The DAT pathway is roughly an order of magnitude faster than the NET pathway: in mice, dopamine concentrations decay with a half-life of 200 milliseconds in the
      <a href="/wiki/Caudate_nucleus" title="Caudate nucleus">
       caudate nucleus
      </a>
      (which uses the DAT pathway) versus 2,000 milliseconds in the prefrontal cortex.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid17881525_92-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid17881525-92">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        92
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Dopamine that is not broken down by enzymes is repackaged into vesicles for future release.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Chemistry">
       Chemistry
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Dopamine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Chemistry">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tmulti tnone center">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:635px;max-width:635px">
       <div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:252px;max-width:252px">
        <div class="thumbimage">
         <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dopamine2.svg">
          <img alt="Dopamine structure" data-file-height="63" data-file-width="136" height="116" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Dopamine2.svg/250px-Dopamine2.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Dopamine2.svg/375px-Dopamine2.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Dopamine2.svg/500px-Dopamine2.svg.png 2x" width="250"/>
         </a>
        </div>
        <div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">
         Dopamine structure
        </div>
       </div>
       <div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:258px;max-width:258px">
        <div class="thumbimage">
         <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fenyloetyloamina.svg">
          <img alt="Phenethylamine structure" data-file-height="120" data-file-width="265" height="116" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Fenyloetyloamina.svg/256px-Fenyloetyloamina.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Fenyloetyloamina.svg/384px-Fenyloetyloamina.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Fenyloetyloamina.svg/512px-Fenyloetyloamina.svg.png 2x" width="256"/>
         </a>
        </div>
        <div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">
         Phenethylamine structure
        </div>
       </div>
       <div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:119px;max-width:119px">
        <div class="thumbimage">
         <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Brenzcatechin.svg">
          <img alt="Catechol structure" data-file-height="128" data-file-width="129" height="116" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Brenzcatechin.svg/117px-Brenzcatechin.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Brenzcatechin.svg/176px-Brenzcatechin.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Brenzcatechin.svg/234px-Brenzcatechin.svg.png 2x" width="117"/>
         </a>
        </div>
        <div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">
         Catechol structure
        </div>
       </div>
       <div style="clear:left">
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Chemically, a dopamine molecule consists of a
      <a href="/wiki/Catechol" title="Catechol">
       catechol
      </a>
      structure (a
      <a href="/wiki/Benzene" title="Benzene">
       benzene
      </a>
      ring with two
      <a href="/wiki/Hydroxyl" title="Hydroxyl">
       hydroxyl
      </a>
      side groups) with one
      <a href="/wiki/Amine" title="Amine">
       amine
      </a>
      group attached. As such, dopamine is the simplest possible
      <a href="/wiki/Catecholamine" title="Catecholamine">
       catecholamine
      </a>
      , a family that also includes the
      <a href="/wiki/Neurotransmitter" title="Neurotransmitter">
       neurotransmitters
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Norepinephrine" title="Norepinephrine">
       norepinephrine
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Epinephrine" title="Epinephrine">
       epinephrine
      </a>
      . The presence of a benzene ring with an attached amine group makes it a
      <a href="/wiki/Phenethylamine" title="Phenethylamine">
       phenethylamine
      </a>
      , a family that includes numerous psychoactive drugs.
     </p>
     <p>
      Dopamine, like most
      <a href="/wiki/Amine" title="Amine">
       amines
      </a>
      , is an
      <a href="/wiki/Organic_base" title="Organic base">
       organic base
      </a>
      . At neutral or acidic pH levels it is generally
      <a href="/wiki/Protonation" title="Protonation">
       protonated
      </a>
      . The protonated form is highly water-soluble and relatively stable, though it is capable of oxidizing if exposed to oxygen or other oxidants. At basic pH levels, dopamine becomes deprotonated. In this
      <a href="/wiki/Free_base" title="Free base">
       free base
      </a>
      form it is less soluble and also highly reactive and easily oxidized. Because of this pH-dependence, dopamine is supplied for chemical or pharmaceutical use in the form of dopamine hydochloride, that is, the
      <a href="/wiki/Hydrochloride" title="Hydrochloride">
       hydrochloride
      </a>
      salt that is created when dopamine is combined with hydrochloric acid. In dry form, dopamine hydrochloride is a fine colorless powder. When dissolved in distilled water it gives a solution that is mildly acidic and therefore relatively stable. It cannot, however, be combined with alkaline solutions such as a
      <a href="/wiki/Bicarbonate" title="Bicarbonate">
       bicarbonate
      </a>
      buffer without being rendered inactive.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Oxidation">
       Oxidation
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Dopamine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Oxidation">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Dopamine in the body is normally broken down by oxidation catalyzed by the enzyme
      <a href="/wiki/Monoamine_oxidase" title="Monoamine oxidase">
       monoamine oxidase
      </a>
      . However, dopamine is also capable of
      <a href="/wiki/Autoxidation" title="Autoxidation">
       autoxidation
      </a>
      , that is, direct reaction with oxygen, yielding
      <a href="/wiki/Quinone" title="Quinone">
       quinones
      </a>
      plus various
      <a href="/wiki/Radical_(chemistry)" title="Radical (chemistry)">
       free radicals
      </a>
      as products.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sulzer_93-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Sulzer-93">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        93
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The rate of autoxidation can be increased by the presence of ferrous iron or other factors. The ability of dopamine autoxidation to produce quinones and free radicals makes it a potent cell toxin, and there is evidence that this mechanism may contribute to cell loss that occurs in
      <a href="/wiki/Parkinson%27s_disease" title="Parkinson's disease">
       Parkinson's disease
      </a>
      or other conditions.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94">
       <a href="#cite_note-94">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        94
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Polydopamine">
       <span id="Polydopamine">
       </span>
       Polydopamine
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Dopamine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Polydopamine">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Research motivated by mussel adhesive proteins led to the discovery in 2007 that a wide variety of materials, if placed in a solution of dopamine at slightly basic pH, will become coated with a layer of polymerized dopamine, often referred to as
      <b>
       polydopamine
      </b>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95">
       <a href="#cite_note-95">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        95
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dreyer_96-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dreyer-96">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        96
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
